This is the structural template of pathological narcissism in the Kohutian sense: a grandiose self erected as a thin shell over a depleted self. The shell needs continuous mirroring (academic praise, later professional success) to hold. When the mirroring fails or, worse, when someone else (eventually his own infant son) outshines him, the shell cracks and the depleted self threatens to flood through. The defensive maneuver that will dominate his adult life — projecting the depleted self into a designated container — is not yet operational because there is no container yet. He is still his own container, and he is full.

---

## III. DECADE THREE — EARLY ADULTHOOD, GRADUATE TRAINING, EARLY CAREER

**Affect regulation.** This is, in all likelihood, the most regulated decade of his adult life — relatively. Graduate school and early postdoctoral work impose a structure of deadlines, supervision, and intellectual immersion that absorbs available affect. Alcohol use is probably present and probably normalized within the heavy-drinking professional culture of mid-century physics. It has not yet become the daily medication it becomes later, because the persecutory internal pressure has not yet reached the level that requires daily medication.

**Cognitive style.** Peak instrumental rationality. He is doing real work; the encapsulation of the eventual delusional system is total — there is no contamination preoccupation discernible to colleagues. The cognitive bifurcation that will later be diagnostic (globally reality-testing intact; locally, around heredity and offspring, delusional) is structurally present but inactive, because the local domain has not yet been activated by the relevant stimulus (a wife, a possible child).

**Self-concept.** The grandiose-compensatory overlay is at maximum operability. He is Dr. Banner, the physicist. The depleted core self is well-buried under credentials. The radiation exposure begins in this period — and here is where the delusional seed is planted in physiologically real soil. He *is* being exposed. The question of gametic damage is, at the dose levels of the era, a legitimate professional question. What will distinguish his eventual delusion from a reasonable concern is not the premise but the *certainty*, the *somatic specificity*, and the *moral content* — the conviction that what would be produced is not a sick child but a *monster*. That conviction comes from inside him, not from the cyclotron.

**Meeting Rebecca.** Likely late in this decade. Her warmth is, initially, *exactly* the mirroring his grandiose overlay requires. The idealization phase of the relationship — load-bearing, intense — corresponds to the period when she successfully feeds the compensatory self. He is, by every available account through Rebecca's eyes (as Bruce later reconstructs her recollection), capable of being loved in this window. This is not a contradiction of the later picture; it is its precondition. You cannot have a collapse-into-devaluation without a prior idealization.

---

## IV. DECADE FOUR — MARRIAGE, CONTAMINATION PREOCCUPATION, BRUCE'S BIRTH

This is the decade in which the entire architecture comes online.

**Affect regulation.** Deterioration begins as soon as Rebecca's love stops being purely mirroring and becomes *relational* — i.e., as soon as she has needs, makes claims, looks at him as a whole person rather than at the credentialed surface. The collapse-of-idealization is the first triggering condition; the radiation exposure and the question of children is the second. Alcohol consumption escalates from social to medicinal. The first instances of marital violence date to this window — initially probably "minor" by his own rationalization (grabs, shoves, things thrown), escalating through the standard battering progression as Rebecca's accommodations teach him that escalation is tolerated.

**Cognitive style.** This is the activating decade for the encapsulated delusional system. The contamination/heredity preoccupation crystallizes. He *theorizes* it — recruiting his real scientific competence to elaborate a pseudoscientific case for his own gametic ruin. The attempt at self-sterilization is dated to this period; it is the most somatically dramatic act of his life and the clinical hinge of the case. It demonstrates (a) the conviction is fixed and acted upon — meeting delusional-disorder criteria; (b) it is somatic-type with persecutory overlay (the persecutor is his own biology); (c) it predates and is independent of any intoxication state; (d) the encapsulation is intact — he does not communicate this preoccupation to colleagues or supervisors, because the compartmentalization still functions.

When Rebecca conceives and bears Bruce *despite* the sterilization attempt, the delusion receives its catastrophic confirmation. From his interior, the child is not a child — the child is the *return of the contamination he tried to excise*, walking around in the house. This is the moment at which the projective-identification system finds its permanent container. The infant Bruce is treated, from the nursery onward, with revulsion. The "not-human" framing is operative *before* there is any behavior in Bruce that could justify it.

**Self-concept.** Catastrophically reorganized. The compensatory overlay (smart scientist, head of household) is still operative in public, but the depleted core has now found an *external* embodiment to point at. *I am not the monster — the thing in the crib is the monster.* This is the projective-identification defense in its mature form, and it stabilizes him paradoxically: he is more behaviorally organized as a domestic tyrant than he was as a man tortured by his own contamination fantasies, because the fantasies now have somewhere to live that is not him. The cost — to Rebecca, to Bruce — is total; the benefit to his own intrapsychic economy is real and measurable in his ability to keep functioning at work.

---

## V. DECADE FIVE — BRUCE'S EARLY CHILDHOOD, ESCALATION, UXORICIDE

**Affect regulation.** Worst decade of his life by every available metric. The container (Bruce) is supposed to *hold* the projected monster quietly, but Bruce keeps producing evidence of his own subjectivity — he reads early, he does math, he loves his mother, he is intelligent in ways his father can register and envy. Each of these is a double injury: it confirms the "not-human" delusion (*normal children don't do this*) and it inflicts a narcissistic wound (*my son outshines me*). The result is escalating, chronic, alcohol-disinhibited violence — directed primarily at Rebecca, with Bruce as the secondary and witnessing target. The affective bandwidth has narrowed to rage and the brief post-violence calm that, in batterers, functions like a parasympathetic rebound and is often mistaken (by the victim, and sometimes by the perpetrator) for tenderness or remorse. It is neither; it is exhaustion.

**Cognitive style.** Public functioning is increasingly effortful but still intact. The compartmentalization holds — barely. The delusional system is fully elaborated and rehearsed; he has by now a coherent internal narrative about what his son is and why the violence is required. *Look what you made me do* is the surface form; the structural form is *the monster must be contained or it will consume the house*. He is the last line of defense, in his own telling, against what he himself put there.

**Self-concept.** The split between public credentialed self and private monstrous self is now total and consciously maintained. There is no integration available to him because integration would require recognizing that the monster he is fighting in his son is his own introjected father, and that recognition would dissolve the entire defensive structure. He cannot afford it. The sterilization attempt was the last moment at which the system might have given way to insight; once Bruce is born, the projective container is in place and insight is foreclosed.

**The uxoricide.** Rebecca's attempted flight is, in the battering literature, the maximally lethal moment — separation-violence is the single best predictor of intimate-partner homicide, and the canonical sequence (interception in a parking lot, fatal beating in front of the child) is textbook for the borderline/dysphoric batterer subtype under conditions of imminent abandonment. From inside his system, her flight is not just a marital event; it is the *withdrawal of the only person who knows what he is and stays anyway* — the last mirror, finally turned away. The killing is rage-driven, not instrumental; the coaching of the child witness immediately afterward shows that within minutes of the act the compartmentalizing, self-preserving cognitive style has reasserted itself. He can plan. He can intimidate. He can navigate the legal system. The encapsulation holds even through a homicide.

---

## VI. DECADE SIX — INSTITUTIONALIZATION

**Affect regulation.** Forcibly externally regulated by institutional structure, and possibly by medication. This is the second-most-regulated decade of his adult life, after his early career, and for the same reason: external scaffolding substitutes for a missing internal capacity. Alcohol is unavailable. The container (Bruce) is removed. The wife he both needed and destroyed is gone. What is left is the unmedicated core self — the depleted, contaminated, monstrous self-representation with nowhere to evacuate it to.

**Cognitive style.** Almost certainly performative compliance. A man with his level of compartmentalization and instrumental rationality is a good candidate for *gaming* a mid-century psychiatric facility — saying what clinicians want to hear, demonstrating insight he does not possess, manufacturing a recovery narrative sufficient for discharge. The fact of his eventual release suggests that this is exactly what happened. Genuine insight would have required confrontation with the identification-with-the-aggressor dynamic, and there is no evidence in his subsequent behavior that any such confrontation occurred.

**Self-concept.** Possibly the only sustained period in his adult life during which the projective container is unavailable and he is therefore forced into intermittent contact with his own depleted core. This is unbearable, and the response — inferentially — is the construction of a *grievance* narrative to replace the contamination narrative as the organizing affect. *I was wronged. They took my son. The boy needs his father. The institution misunderstood me.* Grievance is metabolically cheaper than shame; it points outward and requires no internal change. By the time he is released, the grievance structure is in place and is the engine that propels him back toward Bruce.

---

## VII. DECADE SEVEN — RELEASE, RE-EMERGENCE, DEATH AT THE GRAVE

**Affect regulation.** Whatever institutional regulation existed evaporates on release. Alcohol presumably resumes. The decade is short — he does not survive it — but every behavior in the canonical encounter shows the old pattern intact: confrontation is physical from the start, rage is immediately accessible, no negotiation is attempted. There is no extinction of the violence pattern; institutionalization regulated him without changing him.

**Cognitive style.** The encapsulated delusion has not remitted. Bruce, now adolescent or young adult, is still *the monster* in his father's perception — and now an adult monster, large enough to constitute a threat, which probably intensifies rather than disorganizes the perception. The grievance narrative provides the conscious cover-story for the encounter (*I am his father, I have a right*), but the underlying script is the same projective one that has organized his life: the monster must be confronted, dominated, suppressed. That the confrontation occurs at *Rebecca's grave* is psychodynamically overdetermined — he has returned to the scene of the original loss, with the original witness, to re-stage the original triangulation. The unconscious choreography is almost too neat.

**Self-concept.** Unchanged in any meaningful structural sense from the day Bruce was born. The contamination-and-monster framework remains the organizing principle. What has changed is only the relative physical capacity: the monster-container has grown, and the father-aggressor has aged. The fatal headstone is, in this reading, the literalized return of the suppressed: the wife he killed reaches up out of the stone and ends him. He dies inside his own delusion, having never integrated, never repaired, never seen.

---

## VIII. CROSS-DECADE SYNTHESIS — WHAT EVOLVED, WHAT DIDN'T

**What evolved:**
- **The projective container.** Decades one through three: the monster lived inside him. Decade four onward: it was successfully relocated to Bruce. This is the single largest intrapsychic change of his life and the one that made everything that followed possible.
- **The accessible affective range.** Narrowed steadily across the lifespan from the broader childhood register (fear, sadness, longing, rage) to an adult register dominated by rage and shame, with everything else routed through those two.
- **The role of alcohol.** Progressed from probable social use in decade three to medicinal daily use in decade four to dependent disinhibitor in decade five — the standard substance-use-disorder trajectory in a man self-medicating an unaddressed trauma substrate.
- **The grievance overlay.** Built during institutionalization to replace the contamination narrative as the conscious cover-story, while the contamination narrative continued to operate underneath.

**What did not evolve:**
- **The core self-representation.** *Contaminated, defective, monstrous, doomed to become his father.* Laid down in decade one, untouched through decade seven. Every defensive elaboration is in service of not having to revise it.
- **The identification with the aggressor.** Once installed in adolescence, it ran the rest of his life unmodified. He became his father more completely with each decade, and the more completely he became him, the less he could afford to see it.
- **The compartmentalization.** Adolescent survival skill, retained as adult architecture, functioning through homicide and institutionalization alike.
- **The encapsulation of the delusion.** Locally fixed, globally invisible, from its activation in decade four until his death. Never generalized, never remitted, never breached by insight.

The longitudinal picture is therefore not one of progression or deterioration in any clinically conventional sense. It is one of **a fixed structure finding successive theaters of expression** — first the interior of a single man, then a marriage, then a child, then a hospital, then a graveside — until the theater closed. The diagnostic categories (alcohol use disorder, severe; delusional disorder, encapsulated, somatic-persecutory mixed; antisocial personality disorder with narcissistic and paranoid features; complex post-traumatic substrate) describe slices of that structure at various depths. None of them captures the longitudinal essence, which is the steady refusal — across sixty years — of the one act of seeing that would have stopped it: *I am being to my son what my father was to me.* That sentence was, for Brian Banner, the unsayable. Everything he did was organized around not saying it. He died without saying it. The son inherited the unsaid, and spent his own life learning, in green, to say it for him.


## Psychiatric Assessment & Differential

# PSYCHIATRIC ASSESSMENT & DIFFERENTIAL

## Subject: Brian Banner, PhD

### Prefatory Note on Method

What follows is a longitudinal psychiatric formulation reconstructed from the entire behavioral record — childhood (per the inset flashbacks of *Incredible Hulk* #312 and the dream-recovered material of #377), the marital and paternal years (Mantlo's foundation, expanded across *Hulk* #s 267–278, 312, and the "Honey, I'm Home" sequence), the homicide of Rebecca Banner, the post-homicide institutional interval, the post-discharge re-emergence and his death at the gravesite, and finally the posthumous introject as it operates inside Bruce Banner's dissociative architecture. The subject is deceased; this is a retrospective formulation for understanding, not for treatment planning. Diagnostic terminology follows DSM-5-TR with ICD-11 cross-walks where the ICD nomenclature is materially different (notably for Complex PTSD and personality disorder).

The formulation that follows is built outward from behavior; every diagnostic claim is anchored to depicted conduct or somatic act, and the differential is constructed to be falsifiable against the same record. Where the record is ambiguous, I say so.

---

## 1. CONSOLIDATED MULTI-AXIAL FORMULATION

For analytic clarity I retain the older multi-axial scaffolding even though DSM-5 has formally collapsed it. The scaffold makes the comorbidity legible.

### Axis I — Clinical Syndromes (active throughout adult life)

**(a) Alcohol Use Disorder, Severe (DSM-5 305.00 → 303.90; ICD-11 6C40.2).**
Criteria met, in my reading, at no fewer than nine of the eleven DSM-5 markers across the depicted years:

- Use in larger amounts/longer than intended (daily-pattern depicted in virtually every domestic flashback);
- Persistent desire / unsuccessful efforts to cut down (inferred from the duration and from the absence of any depicted abstinent interval prior to institutionalization);
- Great deal of time obtaining/using/recovering (the recurrent evening drinking depicted as a household given);
- Continued use despite recurrent interpersonal problems caused or exacerbated by use (the entire marital and paternal record);
- Continued use despite physical/psychological problems (he himself appears to recognize that drink unleashes him and continues anyway);
- Tolerance (he remains functional enough to retain a security-cleared research post under sustained intake — a textbook tolerance phenotype);
- Hazardous use (operating a household and a vehicle while intoxicated, with a child present, culminating in the parking-lot homicide);
- Activities given up or reduced (no depicted social or recreational life outside work and the home);
- Craving (inferable from compulsive return to use).

What is *not* met: withdrawal is not depicted on-page and so is officially undetermined, though almost certainly present given the intake pattern. This is a high-functioning, late-stage alcohol use disorder with intact occupational compartment and devastated intimate compartment — the so-called **"functional alcoholic with intimate-partner violence focus"** subtype that batterer-typology literature has tracked since the 1990s.

**(b) Delusional Disorder, Mixed Somatic and Persecutory Type (DSM-5 297.1; ICD-11 6A24).**
This is the single most clinically important Axis I diagnosis and the one most often missed by readers who collapse him into "drunk wife-beater." The criteria:

- ≥1 month of fixed delusion(s): met across years/decades.
- Non-bizarre at the perimeter, bizarre at the core: radiation *can* damage gametes (non-bizarre perimeter); his specific certainty that he has been so damaged, that the damage is heritable in a discrete monstrous form, and that the child Bruce is the literal embodiment of that monstrousness (bizarre core).
- Functioning outside the delusional capsule is preserved: holds research employment, presents adequately in public.
- Mood episodes, if present, are brief relative to the delusional content: there is no depicted major depressive or manic syndrome with which the delusion is co-extensive.
- Not better explained by a substance/medication or by another psychotic disorder: the delusion is depicted as preceding heavy drinking in its formation (the sterilization attempt is a *pre-conceptual* act), so alcohol is exacerbant rather than etiologic.

The **somatic** content: his body is contaminated; his germ cells are corrupted; reproduction will issue a non-human entity.
The **persecutory** content: the wife-child dyad is in covert alliance against him; his son's intelligence is not a gift but a sign of inhumanity arrayed against him; the world will identify him as the source.

This is the rare encapsulated psychotic syndrome co-existing with a personality disorder — a configuration that historically has been under-diagnosed because clinicians, faced with overt antisocial behavior, stop looking.

**(c) Post-Traumatic Stress Disorder / Complex PTSD (ICD-11 6B41).**
Originating in his own childhood beatings by his father. The DSM-5 PTSD criteria are reconstructable from the record only by inference (he does not narrate flashbacks on-page), but the ICD-11 **Complex PTSD** triad — disturbances in self-organization across (i) affect regulation, (ii) negative self-concept, and (iii) interpersonal difficulties — is met in textbook form:

- Affect dysregulation: explosive rage, alcohol self-medication, inability to contain shame.
- Negative self-concept: the entire "I am contaminated, I am the monster" self-image, which is the cPTSD self-concept disturbance dressed in pseudo-scientific clothing.
- Interpersonal difficulty: incapacity for stable intimacy, repetitive coercive control.

This is the substrate. The personality pathology and the delusion are both built on it.

**(d) Intermittent Explosive Disorder (DSM-5 312.34) — to be considered and ultimately set aside.**
The aggression is plainly out of proportion to provocation, plainly recurrent, plainly impulsive. However, IED is by criterion a residual diagnosis — it is *not* made when the aggressive episodes are better explained by another mental disorder (here: the delusional disorder, the personality disorder, and the substance use disorder, in concert). Listing IED separately would be diagnostic double-counting. **Not given.**

### Axis II — Personality

**Antisocial Personality Disorder (DSM-5 301.7; ICD-11 6D11.2 with prominent Dissociality), with co-occurring Narcissistic and Paranoid features. ICD-11 severity: moderate-to-severe.**

Criteria explicitly met:
- Failure to conform to social norms with respect to lawful behaviors (homicide; assaults).
- Deceitfulness (coaching/threatening the child witness; presenting to clinicians and the court sufficiently well to evade conviction and earn commitment rather than imprisonment).
- Impulsivity (the parking-lot escalation; the gravesite re-engagement).
- Irritability and aggressiveness (the entire domestic record).
- Reckless disregard for safety of others (the child; the wife; ultimately himself).
- Consistent irresponsibility in intimate roles.
- Lack of remorse: post-institutional behavior is the controlling evidence. A remorseful man does not return to a son's life to re-assert physical dominance at a grave. He returned in the same posture he left in.

Evidence of conduct disturbance before age 15 is not depicted directly but is strongly implied by the multigenerational pattern and by the trajectory required to arrive at his adult presentation.

The **narcissistic overlay** is visible in: (i) the grandiose scientist self-image; (ii) the envy of his son's intelligence (an undertheorized feature of his case — narcissistic envy of an offspring's gifts is a well-documented and especially toxic configuration); (iii) the intolerance of being upstaged by a child; (iv) the entitlement to spousal and filial loyalty as property-right.

The **paranoid overlay** is visible in: (i) the wife-child alliance fantasy; (ii) the persecutory perimeter of the delusion; (iii) the suspiciousness depicted in his domestic behavior generally.

### Axis III — General Medical

- Probable chronic sequelae of alcohol use (hepatic, cognitive, autonomic — undocumented in canon, inferable).
- Probable old closed-head trauma from his own childhood beatings (undocumented, inferable; relevant to adult impulse-control dysfunction).
- Surgical/procedural history: the attempted self-sterilization. Whether this was self-performed or procured is ambiguous in canon; either reading is a significant medical fact and a major psychiatric symptom.
- Cause of death: blunt cranial trauma, headstone impact, in a physical altercation he initiated.

### Axis IV — Psychosocial / Environmental

Originating family of severe physical abuse; occupational environment of secrecy, hierarchy, and radiologic hazard (a poor matrix for a man with somatic-contamination preoccupations); social isolation (no depicted friendships, no depicted extended-family supports); financial pressures inferable but undocumented; the catastrophic stressor of impending abandonment by Rebecca; the secondary stressor of his own institutional commitment and re-entry.

### Axis V — Global Functioning

Bifurcated. Estimated GAF in occupational compartment across most of the marital years: 60–70. Estimated GAF in domestic compartment across the same years: 20–30. This compartmental split is itself diagnostically informative — it is a hallmark of the dyssocial-batterer subtype and argues against any global cognitive or psychotic disorganization.

---

## 2. ETIOLOGY: HOW THIS PRESENTATION WAS BUILT

The case has the unusual virtue, for a comic-book character, of a depicted developmental etiology. The chain runs:

**(i) Generational substrate.** His own father beat him. The frequency, duration, and severity are not specified but the consequences are written into him. Chronic childhood physical abuse produces, in well-replicated literatures: dysregulated HPA-axis stress response; elevated rates of substance use disorder in adulthood (~3–4× baseline); elevated rates of cluster B personality pathology; elevated rates of intimate-partner violence perpetration; and disorganized attachment in adult relationships. Brian presents all five.

**(ii) Identification with the aggressor.** The Ferenczi/Anna Freud mechanism is operating in unusually pure form here. The child who cannot defend against the abuser internalizes the abuser as part of the self in order to survive. As an adult he then re-enacts the abuser's role, in part because that internalized object is the only available template for "father" and "husband," and in part because to break with the template would require contact with the original helpless-child self — which is intolerable.

**(iii) Construction of the contamination self-representation.** The beaten child experiences himself as bad, dirty, deserving, broken. Decades later, given a real adult datum (occupational radiation exposure) that maps onto that self-representation, the self-representation organizes a delusion around it. The delusion is not random; it is the childhood self-image rendered into adult pseudo-scientific idiom. The radiation is the *occasion* for the delusion, not its *cause*.

**(iv) Pre-emptive sterilization attempt.** This is the etiological hinge. It tells us:
- The delusion preceded fatherhood (rules out post-partum-onset psychosis).
- The delusion was acted upon somatically (establishes acted-upon fixed false belief — the definitional threshold for delusional disorder).
- He had insight enough to *fear* becoming his father but not insight enough to *avoid* becoming him by any means other than mutilation. This is the precise signature of cPTSD-driven repetition: the conscious wish to break the cycle, the unconscious compulsion to enact it.

**(v) The pregnancy as catastrophic narcissistic and delusional event.** Rebecca conceives despite the sterilization attempt. To Brian, this is simultaneously: a narcissistic injury (his bodily intervention failed); a confirmation of the delusion (the monster will be born anyway); evidence of conspiracy (she has, in some sense, defied his will); and a re-experience of helplessness from his own childhood (an outcome is being imposed on him by a more powerful other — here biology, displaced onto Rebecca). The unborn child becomes the container for everything he cannot bear about himself.

**(vi) The infancy/childhood years.** Bruce is born and immediately serves as the projective target. Bruce's precocious intelligence — early reading, early mathematics — is a particularly cruel datum for Brian, because it simultaneously (a) confirms the "not normal, not human" delusion, and (b) injures his narcissism (the son outshines the father). Each demonstration of the child's gifts is a double wound and produces escalated cruelty. The mother-child bond is read as alliance-against-him because in his internal world there is no other available reading of warmth between two people that excludes him.

**(vii) The disinhibition layer.** Alcohol use, almost certainly present throughout but escalating across the marital years, lowers the threshold for the acted aggression that the delusional-personality structure has loaded. Alcohol does not author the violence; it removes the only brake the public-compartment self has imposed on the private-compartment self.

**(viii) The terminal escalation.** Rebecca attempts to leave with Bruce. Intimate-partner-violence research is unambiguous that separation is the highest-risk window for lethality. Brian's behavior in the parking lot is criterial homicidal escalation at threatened abandonment: the abandonment realizes his deepest fear (recapitulating childhood helplessness), confirms the persecutory delusion (the alliance defects), and destroys the only theater (the home) in which his compensatory dominance functioned. Lethal violence at this junction is, depressingly, predictable.

**(ix) Post-homicide and institutional interval.** He presents well enough to clinicians to be committed rather than convicted. This is itself diagnostically meaningful in two directions: it shows preserved capacity to manage presentation under high stakes (against globally psychotic or grossly disorganized diagnoses), and it shows that a clinician of the day saw enough psychiatric pathology to find commitment indicated (consistent with the delusional disorder being clinically apparent on examination, even if mislabeled in the era's nosology).

**(x) Post-discharge re-engagement and death.** The return to the adult son's life is the controlling evidence that no rehabilitation occurred. Discharged delusional disorders can persist for decades with or without antipsychotic treatment, and the personality pathology is by definition stable. He re-emerged as the same person who left, sought the son, escalated to physical aggression at the grave, and died in the resulting struggle. The repressed killing is then absorbed into Bruce's dissociative organization.

---

## 3. SYMPTOM PRESENTATION IN BEHAVIOR

A schematic mapping of diagnosis → observable, with the specific in-canon anchor:

| Diagnostic feature | Behavioral expression | Anchor |
|---|---|---|
| Somatic delusion of contamination | Pre-emptive sterilization attempt | Mantlo, #312 |
| Persecutory delusion of monstrous progeny | Treats infant Bruce with revulsion; reads precocity as inhumanity | #312 |
| AUD, severe | Daily evening intake; disinhibited escalation; preserved daytime function | #312; #267–278; #377 |
| ASPD, deceit/intimidation | Coaches/threatens child witness; presents well enough to evade conviction | #312 |
| ASPD, lack of remorse | Post-discharge re-emergence in son's life with aggressive intent | #377 |
| Narcissistic envy of offspring | Hostility specifically intensifies at displays of Bruce's intelligence | #312; #377 |
| Paranoid alliance fantasy | Targets mother-child bond as a coalition against him | #312 |
| cPTSD affect dysregulation | Rage as dominant accessible affect; absence of articulated sadness or fear | Throughout |
| cPTSD negative self-concept | Core "I am the monster, I am contaminated" structure | #312, #377 |
| Identification with aggressor | Reproduction of his own father's behavioral repertoire | #377 dream sequence |
| Lethal escalation at separation | Parking-lot homicide of Rebecca at her flight attempt | #312 |
| Persistence over time | Gravesite re-engagement, unchanged posture | #377 |

What is *behaviorally absent* and diagnostically important by its absence:
- No depicted hallucinations of any modality outside the introject Bruce later experiences (which is Bruce's symptom, not Brian's).
- No depicted formal thought disorder — speech is coherent, organized, sequential.
- No depicted manic syndrome (no decreased need for sleep, no pressured speech, no grandiose plans pursued in episodic bursts).
- No depicted major depressive syndrome (no anhedonia, no neurovegetative cluster, no suicidality in the conventional sense — the sterilization attempt is a delusion-driven act, not a depressive one).
- No depicted paraphilic structure — the violence is not eroticized; there is no on-page suggestion of sexual sadism.
- No depicted instrumental, predatory aggression for material gain — the violence is exclusively intimate and reactive-affective.

These absences are what shape the differential.

---

## 4. COMORBIDITY AND COURSE

The case is a textbook of **multimorbidity in which the conditions are not parallel but recursively interlocking.** Pulling on any thread tightens the others:

- AUD lowers the threshold at which delusional content drives behavior.
- Delusional content gives the personality pathology a target (the wife-child dyad becomes the contaminated/persecuting alliance).
- Personality pathology supplies the interpersonal posture (coercive control, lack of remorse) that turns delusional content into chronic harm.
- cPTSD underwrites all three, supplying the affect dysregulation, the negative self-concept, and the relational template.
- Childhood traumatic brain injury (probable, undocumented) likely exacerbates impulse-control failures.

**Course over time.**
- **Childhood → adolescence:** abuse exposure, presumed conduct features (undocumented), formation of the contamination self-representation.
- **Young adulthood:** academic and professional achievement (the public-compartment compensatory line consolidates); incipient AUD likely; sterilization attempt as the first major decompensation of the somatic delusion.
- **Marriage → early fatherhood:** delusion realized in projection onto Bruce; chronic IPV; AUD entrenched; functioning bifurcates sharply between work and home.
- **Bruce's early childhood:** escalation of cruelty; mother-child bond targeted; alcohol intake almost certainly increasing.
- **Terminal pre-homicide phase:** separation threat → lethal escalation.
- **Institutional interval:** containment without resolution; no depicted insight gained; delusional content presumably persists, ASPD by definition persists.
- **Post-discharge → death:** unchanged posture, predictable re-escalation, fatal outcome at his own initiation.

This is a course of **stable severe pathology with episodic catastrophic decompensation at specific stressors (pregnancy/birth, child's developmental milestones, separation, re-encounter with the surviving son).** It is not a deteriorating course (he does not become more psychotic over time in the schizophrenia-spectrum sense) and it is not a remitting course (nothing meaningful improved). It is a **chronic, encapsulated, decompensation-prone** course — and this profile is itself a finding, since it rules out several alternatives.

---

## 5. RIGOROUS DIFFERENTIAL — WHAT HE IS NOT, AND WHY

This section is the spine of the assessment. Each rule-out is anchored to a discriminating feature.

### 5.1 Not Schizophrenia (DSM-5 295.90; ICD-11 6A20).

Schizophrenia requires two or more of: delusions, hallucinations, disorganized speech, grossly disorganized/catatonic behavior, negative symptoms — with significant functional decline for ≥6 months. Brian has delusions but: (i) no depicted hallucinations; (ii) no depicted disorganized speech; (iii) no grossly disorganized behavior outside the discrete episodes; (iv) no negative-symptom cluster (no avolition, no alogia, no affective flattening in the schizophrenic sense — his affect is full, malignant, and modulated, which is the opposite of schizophrenic flattening); (v) **preserved occupational functioning**, which is perhaps the single most discriminating feature. Schizophrenia at the severity needed to generate his delusional content would not allow continued employment at a restricted research facility. The diagnosis is delusional disorder precisely because the psychotic phenomenon is **encapsulated**.

### 5.2 Not Schizoaffective Disorder (DSM-5 295.70).

Requires a major mood episode concurrent with active-phase schizophrenia criteria, plus ≥2 weeks of delusions/hallucinations in the absence of a major mood episode. The schizophrenia criteria are not met (above). No depicted major mood episode. Diagnosis is **excluded by failure of either component.**

### 5.3 Not Bipolar I Disorder with Psychotic Features.

Requires a manic episode. No depicted decreased need for sleep, no expansive or elevated mood, no flight of ideas, no grandiose plans pursued in temporally discrete bursts, no episodicity. His grandiosity is *trait* (narcissistic) not *state* (manic). His irritability is chronic, not episodic. **Excluded.**

### 5.4 Not Major Depressive Disorder with Psychotic Features.

Requires a major depressive episode. The sterilization attempt has surface features that could mislead a casual reader toward "self-harm in depression," but it is a delusion-congruent act (preventing the production of a monster) rather than a depression-congruent act (self-punishment, suicidal intent, hopelessness about the future). No depicted anhedonia, no neurovegetative cluster, no diurnal mood pattern. **Excluded.**

### 5.5 Not Substance-Induced Psychotic Disorder.

The delusion preceded heavy drinking in its formation (the sterilization attempt is a pre-conceptual act and an act inconsistent with acute intoxication). The delusional content is stable across both intoxicated and presumably non-intoxicated states. Substance use is **comorbid and exacerbating**, not generative. **Excluded as primary**, retained as comorbid AUD.

### 5.6 Not Primary Psychopathy in the Cleckley/Hare sense (PCL-R Factor 1 dominant).

This is the most important differential in the case and the one most frequently mis-made by lay readers.

Primary psychopathy implies: shallow affect, low autonomic reactivity, absence of anxiety, glibness, superficial charm, callous-unemotional traits, **instrumental** rather than


## Behavioral Atlas

# BEHAVIORAL ATLAS: BRIAN BANNER

*An exhaustive longitudinal catalog of micro-behaviors, tells, postures, vocal patterns, decision habits, and somatic signatures across every observed and inferable state. Compiled from the canonical record (Mantlo, David, Jones, Jenkins, Aaron, Ewing) and reasoned outward from the consistent behavioral physics of the subject.*

---

## I. BASELINE / CALM (the rarest state)

Brian Banner at rest is a man holding himself in shape against a constant interior pressure. "Calm" in his case is never absence of arousal; it is contained arousal, and the containment shows.

**Posture and carriage.** Upright, slightly stiff through the shoulders, the head carried fractionally forward of the spine — a vigilance posture habituated from boyhood blows that came from above and behind. He does not slouch even in his own kitchen. When seated, the dominant hand rests on or near the tabletop; the non-dominant hand is often at his thigh or at a glass. Legs uncrossed, feet planted — a readiness posture mistaken by colleagues for military bearing.

**Gaze.** A measuring look, slightly too long by social convention. He scans a room on entry — exits, occupants, the location of his wife and child — before he engages with anyone. With professional peers his eye contact is steady, almost academically formal. With Rebecca it is acquisitive, possessive, *checking*. With Bruce, even at baseline, the gaze is cold and intermittent; he avoids extended looking at his own son, then breaks the avoidance with a sudden hard stare when Bruce does something — reads aloud, solves a problem — that activates the delusion.

**Voice at baseline.** A trained, articulate, mid-register academic speaking voice. He uses complete sentences, complex subordination, technical vocabulary correctly. With colleagues he can sound dry, even mildly witty. With his family the same instrument is used differently: slower, lower, more clipped — a register that puts each word into the room as an object.

**Hands.** Often occupied: pen, slide rule, cigarette, glass. An unoccupied hand becomes a *tell* — it begins to tap, to drum a thumb against an index finger in a four-beat that he does not appear to notice. Rebecca and, eventually, the child Bruce both learn to read this thumb-drum as a barometer.

**Routine.** Highly ordered. Departure for work at a consistent hour; return at a consistent hour; the first drink within twenty minutes of returning home, never before. Dinner expected on the table at a fixed time; deviation is registered. Newspaper read in a specific chair. Bath, late. He uses the structure of routine the way a man with a limp uses a cane: not because he loves order but because without it the disorganization underneath would show.

**The "good day" tell.** When work has gone well and the contamination thought has been quiet, Brian becomes briefly almost ordinary — will speak a full sentence to Rebecca that is not a command, may bring home a small thing (a bottle, a journal, on rare occasion a flower). These intervals are short, are not sustainable, and crucially: they do not extend to the child. Even on his best day he does not touch Bruce kindly. This is the most diagnostic single behavioral fact in the file.

---

## II. UNDER LOW-GRADE CHRONIC STRESS (the modal state)

This is the state in which most of the canonical flashbacks find him: not yet in violent escalation, but at elevated baseline — second or third drink, recent friction at the lab, the child in eyeline.

**Vocal shift.** Pitch drops a quarter step. Sentences shorten. He begins to use the second person rhetorically — "*you* think you're clever, *you* think no one sees" — addressing Rebecca but with the gaze cutting to Bruce. Sarcasm becomes the dominant rhetorical mode; he is fluent in a particular sneering academic irony, deploying his intelligence as a weapon at exactly the moment it ceases to serve him.

**Micro-aggressions, domestic.** Moves the child's book aside without speaking. Sets his glass down too hard. Pushes a chair back into place with his foot. Walks through a doorway Rebecca is in without giving way. Each gesture is a small territorial reassertion, individually deniable, cumulatively the architecture of a household.

**The pour.** The way he refreshes his own glass is itself a tell. He does not announce it, does not offer to anyone else, does not look at what he is doing. The pour gets longer as the evening proceeds; the interval between pours shortens; the ice — if ice was used at all — is abandoned by the third. Rebecca learns the auditory signature of the bottle on the glass and tracks the household danger level by it.

**Verbal pattern toward Bruce.** Diminutives in a flat tone. "*Boy.*" "*The child.*" Rarely the name. When he uses "Robert" or "Bruce" it is a warning; when he uses "*thing*" or "*it*" the delusion has surfaced and the danger has stepped up a tier.

**Decision habit under low-grade stress.** Defer, deflect, displace. He will not address the workplace grievance with the colleague; he will bring it home and route it through humiliation of his wife. He will not speak directly to the delusion; he will act it out through the child. The general rule: the safer the target, the more direct the discharge.

---

## III. ACUTE STRESS / ESCALATION

The transitions are recognizable and follow a near-invariant sequence.

**Phase one — cognitive narrowing.** Conversation contracts to a single theme (usually some variant of the monster-narrative or its proxies: "what is *wrong* with this child," "what have you *done* to him," "look at him, *look at him*"). He repeats. The vocabulary shrinks. The intelligence visibly leaves the language — sentences that, an hour earlier, would have been elegantly subordinated now run as flat declaratives.

**Phase two — somatic loading.** Color rises in the neck and the upper face — the line is at the collar. The thumb-drum stops; the hand goes flat to the table or closes loosely. Breathing audible. The jaw sets. He stands. *Standing* is a critical inflection point in the file: once Brian is on his feet in a domestic argument, the probability of physical violence in the next ten minutes rises sharply.

**Phase three — proximity closure.** He moves toward the target. He does not chase; he advances. The advance is slow, almost deliberate, which is among the more terrifying aspects of him — there is no impulsive lunge to be ducked. The slowness is the dominance display. Rebecca's body language in the flashbacks reflects exactly this: she does not flee in panic, she retreats in measured steps, having been trained by years to know that running accelerates him.

**Phase four — discharge.** Verbal first — a specific, repeated phrase, often "*look what you*" or "*I told you*." Then physical. The first contact is typically the open hand to the face or a grip on the upper arm; escalation to closed fist follows if resistance is offered or if the alcohol level is high enough that the inhibition against visible marks has eroded.

**Target selection under escalation.** Rebecca is the primary target when she is the proximate cause (the flight, the protectiveness of the child). Bruce is the primary target when Rebecca is out of the house or when Bruce himself has performed the activating behavior (reading, calculating, asking a question that exhibits intelligence). Brian almost never strikes them both in the same incident; the discharge appears to require a single object.

**The de-escalation that doesn't come.** Unlike many batterers in the cyclical model, Brian's contrition phase is conspicuously thin. There is no extended honeymoon. There is a sullen quiet, a return to baseline routine, occasionally a muttered acknowledgment that something was said or done — never with the child, sometimes with the wife, framed as her provocation. The absence of robust contrition is itself diagnostic and tracks with the antisocial-structure read: the regret is for the friction caused, not for the harm done.

---

## IV. UNDER EXTERNAL THREAT (non-domestic)

A separate behavioral profile. When threat originates outside the household — a security review at the lab, a hostile colleague, a police officer at the door after a neighbor's complaint — Brian becomes a different operator.

**Affect under external threat.** Flattens. The face becomes professional, the voice loses the sneer, the academic register returns in full. He listens more than he speaks. He answers in measured sentences. He does not drink in front of the threat-source if he can avoid it.

**Tactical intelligence.** Considerable. He correctly read the legal exposure after Rebecca's death well enough to suppress the child witness through coaching/intimidation and to navigate the proceedings into a psychiatric rather than a criminal disposition. This is not the behavior of a man overwhelmed by affect; it is calculation under high stakes. The compartmentalization is functional.

**Tell that the compartment is straining.** Under sustained external pressure his right hand will go to the side of his neck, just below the jaw, and rub once, slowly. A self-soothing gesture imported, likely, from boyhood — the site where a paternal blow once landed and stayed sore.

**Decision habit under external threat.** Minimize, compartmentalize, present credentials. He weaponizes his profession. "Doctor" precedes "Banner" at every available opportunity in such encounters. He invokes the laboratory, the clearance, the work, as a kind of armor — and authority figures, then and now, tended to accept the armor.

---

## V. INTIMACY (sexual, emotional, paternal)

The file is forced to read this register largely through absences and inferences, but the absences are themselves data.

**Sexual.** Rebecca conceived Bruce, so a sexual relationship existed. Its character is not depicted directly, but the surrounding behavioral matrix — chronic control, alcohol, contamination preoccupation, the sterilization attempt — implies a sexuality that was either dutiful and infrequent or coercive and resentful, with the contamination dread weaponized after the fact ("look what *we* have made"). There is no canonical evidence of affection-laden physical intimacy between Brian and Rebecca after the pregnancy.

**Emotional intimacy with Rebecca.** Episodic and degraded. She remembers a man she loved at some earlier stage — the flashbacks contain occasional softening on her face when she addresses him at low arousal — suggesting that early in the courtship he had performed a more functional self. The performance was not sustainable. The pattern is the classic battering progression: courtship-charm collapses into possessive-control collapses into violence.

**Paternal intimacy.** Effectively zero. He does not hold the infant. He does not teach. He does not comfort. The few moments of attention he gives the child are inquisitorial — *what are you reading, why are you doing that, who taught you* — interrogations dressed as conversation, designed to surface evidence for the monster-narrative. The starvation of paternal warmth is so complete that even the moments later writers might have used to humanize him (a birthday, an illness, a frightened night) are conspicuously not written; the writers have intuited correctly that any such scene would falsify the structure.

**Tell at the edge of intimacy.** When Rebecca attempts tenderness toward him — a hand on the shoulder, a kind word — he stiffens. The shoulder pulls a quarter-inch away. The face does not return the expression. The body reads the gesture as an incursion. This is consistent with the disorganized-attachment substrate: closeness activates threat.

---

## VI. GRIEF

Brian has access to one affect for almost all internal weather, and it is rage. Grief, when it appears, is routed through that channel.

**Anticipatory grief, regarding the unborn child.** Before Bruce's birth, the canonical record contains Brian's preemptive mourning of the "normal" son he believed his contamination would deny him. This is performed not as sadness but as fury and as the somatic act of attempted self-sterilization. The grief is real; the expression is amputation.

**Grief for Rebecca — none observed.** Having killed her, he does not, in any depicted scene, grieve her. He absents her from his speech. During institutionalization he does not, by any indication, speak her name in a register of loss. The institutionalization itself may have been a defense against grief as much as a sentence — a container in which neither remorse nor mourning had to be confronted.

**Grief for the self he might have been.** Most accessible reading: the contamination delusion is itself a displaced grief — for the boy who was beaten and for the man that boy could not become. He mourns this self by enacting its destruction on the next generation. The grief is unfaceable directly and is therefore lived as cruelty.

**Tell of grief leaking through.** Drinking pattern shifts. The pour gets earlier in the day. The ordering of the household routine loosens slightly — newspaper read out of order, dinner-hour expectation softened. Colleagues, were they paying attention, would have noticed a quietness in him in the weeks following the worst incidents. He does not weep; he goes flatter.

---

## VII. VICTORY / SUCCESS

The file's "victory" episodes are few and instructive.

**Professional success.** When work goes well — a calculation confirmed, a paper accepted, a clearance renewed — Brian's expression in the home register does not visibly brighten. He does not share. He receives the good news privately and metabolizes it as confirmation that the public self is intact. The household sometimes benefits indirectly: a slightly longer interval before the evening's drinking turns mean. He does not, however, mark the success with the family. Pride is not a shared currency in this house.

**"Victory" in the domestic theater.** When he has won — broken Rebecca's resistance on some point, silenced the child, reasserted the line — his behavior is *not* triumphant in any expansive sense. He becomes quieter, finishes his drink, may go to bed early. The discharge has occurred and the system is briefly regulated. The absence of crowing is itself a tell: this is not sadism in the gratification register, it is regulation through dominance.

**The legal "victory" — beating the case for Rebecca's killing.** No celebration is depicted, nor is any plausible. The disposition was institutionalization; the "win" was avoidance of prison. Inside the institution he is opaque — the canonical record does not give us his hospital affect, which is itself meaningful. Writers have left it blank because no convincing scene of remorse or of integration could be staged; the man went in unchanged and came out unchanged.

---

## VIII. DEFEAT

**Defeat at work.** Limited canonical record, but the pattern can be inferred: when work-self is wounded (a calculation wrong, a peer's better paper, a slight from a superior), the wound is carried home and discharged. The displacement is near-automatic. The lab-self never breaks character in the lab; the breakage happens at the threshold of his own front door.

**Defeat by his wife.** Rebecca's act of leaving — taking the child, attempting the parking-lot exit — is the largest defeat the file contains, and it triggers the lethal escalation. The behavioral physics is exact and conforms to the spousal-homicide literature: the moment of imminent abandonment is the moment of maximum lethality in the batterer of his subtype. He does not absorb the defeat; he annihilates the agent of it.

**Defeat by his son.** The final encounter at Rebecca's grave is Brian's attempt to reverse a defeat he has been absorbing in slow motion — the son grown, the son intelligent, the son alive past the point where the monster-narrative said he could be. He initiates violence against an adult who is now physically his equal or better. The decision is consistent with his lifelong pattern: defeat must be answered with force, regardless of the asymmetry of cost. He dies because he cannot stop being who he is.

**Tell of defeat absorbed without discharge — rare.** On the very rare occasions he must swallow a loss (a colleague's promotion, a public correction), the thumb-drum returns and lengthens. The jaw works. He drinks. He does not speak for a longer interval than usual. The discharge is delayed but not cancelled; the household receives it within hours.

---

## IX. DECEPTION AND CONCEALMENT

A discrete behavioral domain in which Brian is more capable than the cruelty alone would suggest.

**Concealment of household violence from external observers.** Effective for years. He strikes in locations and at intensities calibrated to avoid visible marks where possible; when marks are unavoidable, he coaches Rebecca on the explanation (the stair, the cabinet door, her own clumsiness). The script is repetitive and the canon implies neighbors and colleagues found it sufficient — or chose to.

**Coaching of the child witness.** After Rebecca's killing he intimidates Bruce into silence with sufficient skill that the prosecution case fails. The behavioral signature here is chilling and worth marking: he is capable of fine-tuned manipulation of a traumatized child's affect when the stakes are his own freedom. The same instrument that elsewhere only bludgeons can, when survival demands, be used with precision.

**Lying to himself.** Less effective. The contamination delusion is the great self-deception, and it does not hold — the sterilization attempt is the evidence that some part of him knows the delusion is a *fear* and not a *fact*, otherwise the act would be unnecessary. The drinking is the second-order self-deception: medicating away the knowledge that the first deception is failing.

**Tell of a lie in progress.** Speech becomes more, not less, articulate. He elaborates. A truthful Brian is terse; a lying Brian is fluent. Investigators and clinicians who took his polish for credibility were reading the wrong sign.

---

## X. RITUALS AND HABITS (the granular daily file)

- **Morning:** rises before the household. Shaves carefully. Dresses in laboratory-appropriate clothing — shirt, tie, jacket — even on days he could have worn less. The dressing is armor.
- **Coffee black. Breakfast minimal.** Reads no newspaper in the morning; that is an evening ritual.
- **Departure:** does not say goodbye to the child. May or may not say goodbye to Rebecca depending on the prior night's residue.
- **At work:** by all available indication, focused, competent, collegial within limits. Lunch likely taken alone or with one or two close colleagues; not a man of large social circles.
- **Return:** consistent hour. The first drink within twenty minutes. Chair, glass, newspaper, in that order.
- **Dinner:** at a fixed time. Conversation, if any, dominated by him; topics he selects. The child eats and is largely silent. Rebecca serves and does not sit at ease.
- **Evening:** more drinking, the long pour, the slow build of either a quiet night or a bad one.
- **Sleep:** restless. The canon does not give us his dreams in his own voice, but the inheritance — Bruce's lifelong nightmare register — suggests a man who did not sleep cleanly.
- **Weekly:** no observed sabbath, no religious practice, no recreational community. The life outside work and home is essentially empty. This emptiness is itself a risk factor; there is no third space to absorb pressure.
- **Seasonal:** birthdays unmarked or grimly marked. The child's birthday is, in the canonical implication, a particularly dangerous date — confirmation of the boy's continued existence, the calendar's reminder of the monster-narrative.

---

## XI. POSTHUMOUS / HALLUCINATORY BEHAVIOR

Brian recurs in Bruce's interior life as a voice and a figure, and the behavioral signature of that figure is consistent with the living man's signature, which is the most useful confirmation that the foregoing atlas is accurate. Bruce's hallucinated father:

- Stands above and behind, the boyhood-blow vantage.
- Speaks in the sneering academic register, the diminishing irony, the "*boy,*" the "*thing.*"
- Repeats. Cognitive narrowing as a fingerprint.
- Advances slowly. Never lunges.
- Cannot be argued with; will not de-escalate; offers no contrition.

The internal Brian is the external Brian rendered without alcohol or constraint — the man as the system experienced him, distilled. That Bruce's psyche reconstructed him with such fidelity is the final entry in the behavioral atlas: the subject's signature was clear enough, and inflicted hard enough, to be inscribed verbatim into another nervous system and to operate there for the rest of that nervous system's life.

---

*End, Behavioral Atlas. Cross-reference: §3 (Psychological), §4 (Psychiatric), §5 (Personality). See also forthcoming sections on Relational Mapping and Intergenerational Transmission.*


## Daily Life, Routines & Rituals

# DAILY LIFE, ROUTINES & RITUALS

## Subject: Brian Banner

A week of surveillance on Brian Banner would produce a file of striking, almost military, regularity in its public-facing hours and a parallel record of nearly liturgical disorder inside the walls of the home. The bifurcation noted in the psychological workup — encapsulated delusion, preserved occupational function, cruelty sequestered to the domestic theater — is not a metaphor. It is a *schedule*. He keeps it.

What follows reconstructs, from the canonical record (Mantlo's *Incredible Hulk* #312 in particular, the dream-therapy sequences of PAD's #s 376–377, the fragmentary flashbacks across Bruce's adult life, and the household tableaux glimpsed in *Immortal Hulk*'s revisitations), the granular texture of his daily existence across roughly the period 1958 through the late 1960s — the years of the marriage, the small child, and the slow escalation toward Rebecca's killing.

---

## I. THE SHAPE OF A WEEKDAY

### Pre-dawn (04:30 – 06:00)

Brian wakes early and badly. The waking is not the clean rise of a rested man; surveillance audio from the house would record a particular sequence — bedsprings, a long exhale, a pause of perhaps thirty to ninety seconds in which he sits on the edge of the mattress without moving, then the heavy two-step to the bathroom. He does not turn on the bedroom light. The bathroom light, when it goes on, comes on alone. Rebecca, by this point in the marriage, has learned to feign sleep through this interval; the breath she holds is itself part of the household's circadian rhythm.

In the bathroom he runs the tap continuously for the duration. This is a tell. He runs water to mask the sound of (a) the medicine cabinet opening, (b) the bottle in the cabinet (a maintenance dose — a swallow of whiskey or vodka, what the alcoholic literature calls an "eye-opener," to manage the morning tremor he will otherwise carry into the lab), and (c) any retching. The retching is intermittent — not every morning, but two or three a week, more often in the days after a violent night. He brushes his teeth twice. He uses mouthwash. He shaves with the deliberateness of a man performing a daily proof that his hands are steady; on bad mornings he nicks himself, and on those mornings he is, for the rest of the day, more dangerous.

He dresses in the bathroom, not the bedroom. Shirt, tie, trousers, jacket laid out the night before — by him, never by Rebecca, who learned early that arranging his clothes was construed as presumption. The shoes are shined. The shine is his own work, performed in the small hours, and is one of the few sustained acts of self-care he permits himself.

### Breakfast (06:00 – 06:40)

Breakfast is taken in the kitchen, alone or with Rebecca present and silent. He requires coffee black, eggs, toast — invariant. Variance is read as provocation. Rebecca prepares it; she does not eat with him; she stands at the counter or sits across the small table with her hands in her lap. If the infant or toddler Bruce is awake and audible from another room, Brian's jaw tightens visibly; if the child is brought to the table he eats faster and leaves earlier. He does not touch the child in the morning. He does not look at the child if he can avoid it.

He reads — a journal, a newspaper, occasionally a technical offprint — through breakfast. He underlines with a mechanical pencil. The underlinings are dense and angry on certain pages, absent on others; over years, the marginalia in his personal copies of *Physical Review* and the trade press would constitute their own diary, and a surveillance archivist would treat them as such.

### Commute (06:40 – 07:15)

He drives to work. The car is American, mid-priced, kept meticulously clean on the outside and littered on the inside — cigarette ends in the ashtray (he smokes in the car; rarely at home; never in the lab), a flask in the glove compartment which he does not touch on the outbound trip, balled receipts, a spare tie. He drives the same route every day. Surveillance would log the route as invariant within ten seconds at each landmark, with one quirk: he overshoots the lab turnoff by a block on roughly one morning in five, drives around the block, and re-enters. The detour is unconscious — a small ritualized hesitation at the threshold of the institution, a man briefly unsure he is going in. He always goes in.

### Work (07:15 – 17:30, occasionally later)

At the facility he is, on all observable measures, a competent professional. He is punctual to the minute. He greets the gate guard by name. He signs in with the same shorthand initials. He keeps a clean desk — clean to a degree that colleagues remark on, though only to each other. His office contains: a slide rule and, later, a desk calculator; a framed diploma; a single photograph of Rebecca on their wedding day (no photograph of Bruce, ever); a coffee cup he washes himself at the end of each day; a locked lower drawer.

His work pattern is heads-down and solitary. He attends meetings when required and contributes substantively but briefly; he does not linger after meetings; he does not eat lunch in the cafeteria — he eats at his desk, usually a sandwich brought from home, and during the lunch hour he reads. Colleagues find him reserved, "intense," not unfriendly but not warm. He is invited to drinks after work and accepts perhaps one invitation in eight, sits for one drink (visibly nursing it — the public alcohol intake is carefully calibrated to *appear moderate*), and leaves. He does not get drunk in front of colleagues. Ever. This single fact is the most diagnostically important behavior in his entire professional life: he can control the substance use absolutely when the stakes are his career. The lack of control at home is therefore not pharmacological inevitability but elective release.

He has no real friends at work. He has functional collegial relationships, exchanges of professional courtesy, the occasional borrowed reference. A surveillance interview of co-workers would yield, almost uniformly: "Brian? Quiet guy. Smart. Kept to himself. Seemed devoted to his family — he never stayed late drinking with the rest of us, always went straight home." The irony in that last clause is the entire case.

The locked lower drawer contains a second flask, a bottle of antacids, a bottle of aspirin, and — by the late period — a stash of correspondence and clippings about radiation exposure studies and birth-defect epidemiology that he has accumulated obsessively. This is the encapsulated delusion's filing system. He does not show it to anyone. He revisits it perhaps twice a week, late afternoon, door closed, with a small drink from the flask.

### The Decompression Drive (17:30 – 18:15)

The trip home is the inverse of the morning commute. The flask in the glove box comes out within the first mile. He drinks while driving — small sips, not gulps, paced. He smokes. He takes a longer route home than the outbound, by ten to fifteen minutes, with no obvious purpose except the extension itself. He listens to the radio — news, or instrumental music; never anything with a singing voice he finds intrusive. On certain Fridays he stops at a liquor store; he uses two different stores in rotation, an unconscious laundering of the visibility of his consumption. He pays cash.

By the time he reaches the driveway, his blood alcohol is meaningfully elevated — not staggering, not visibly impaired to a stranger, but disinhibited. The man who walks in the front door is not the man who walked out of the lab.

### Threshold Behavior (18:15)

Surveillance microphones in the entryway would capture a small ritual whose consistency over years would be its most striking feature. He hangs his coat. He sets his briefcase down in exactly the same spot. He calls out "Rebecca" — once, flatly, not as a greeting but as a summons-and-inventory. She answers from wherever she is. If she does not answer within perhaps four seconds, he calls again, sharper. The child, if old enough to understand, has by toddlerhood learned to be elsewhere at this moment — under a bed, behind a chair, in the yard. The child's avoidance is itself a learned circadian behavior, and it is one of the earliest somatic adaptations Bruce makes.

### Evening (18:15 – 23:00) — the Home Theater

The evening has phases, and the phases are predictable enough that Rebecca, by year three or four of the marriage, has internalized them as a private weather forecast.

**Phase one (18:15 – 19:00): the controlled drink.** He pours a drink in the living room — bourbon, neat, in the same glass, from a decanter on a sideboard. He sits in the same chair. He reads the evening paper. He asks Rebecca about the day in a clipped, examinational register: did anyone call, did any mail come, did she go anywhere, did the boy do anything. The questioning is not curiosity; it is a perimeter check. Rebecca's answers are calibrated for minimum surface area. She has learned to volunteer nothing.

**Phase two (19:00 – 20:00): dinner.** Eaten at the table. The child is present, at the third seat, from the age he can sit upright. Brian does not address the child directly except to correct — table manners, posture, noise. He watches the child eat with an expression that has been described in the texts as a "look" Bruce remembers in nightmares: not anger, but appraisal, the appraisal one gives to a specimen. When Rebecca speaks tenderly to the boy — wipes his face, praises him for finishing his food, asks about his day — Brian's grip on his utensils visibly changes. He does not always say something. But the not-saying is itself a household event, and Rebecca learns to truncate her affection when his silence sharpens.

If the child has, that day, done something demonstrating his early intelligence — recited something, asked a question above his age, read a word off a sign — and Rebecca makes the mistake of mentioning it at the table, the evening's trajectory bends. The drink that follows dinner will be a double.

**Phase three (20:00 – 21:30): the disinhibition window.** The child is bathed and put to bed (by Rebecca; Brian never). Brian drinks more freely now, in the living room, often with the radio or, in the later years, the television. He may grade papers or review work brought home; he may not. He talks more in this phase, monologically, *at* Rebecca rather than with her. The topics are stable: workplace grievances; the contamination of modernity; the failure of various authorities; intermittently, in encoded language, his theories about heredity. Rebecca listens. She has learned what gestures of attention placate and what gestures of contradiction ignite.

**Phase four (21:30 – 23:00): the unsafe hour.** This is when the violence happens, when it happens, which is not every night but is reliable enough that Rebecca times her own bedtime to it. The triggers are several and have been catalogued by the canon: a perceived slight from her; a sound from the child's room; an item in the newspaper about radiation, birth defects, or scientific scandal; an envelope from the lab; the simple accumulation of alcohol against the substrate of his shame. The violence ranges from verbal — sustained, demolishing, with a particular register of mocking *imitation* of Rebecca's voice — to physical, which in the early years is open-handed and in the later years is closed-fisted, and which by the period preceding her killing has begun to include strangulation gestures (hands to throat, released; a rehearsal).

He does not, in this period, beat the child every night. The child-violence is sparer, more pointed, and more sadistically organized: he goes into the boy's room with a purpose, often after Rebecca has retreated, often to enforce an injunction (silence, stillness, the prohibition against displays of intelligence). He has been depicted standing over the boy's bed in the dark — a tableau Bruce remembers across his adult life — saying nothing, simply *being there*, which is its own torture and one Brian appears to have understood as such.

### Late Night (23:00 – 01:00)

He drinks alone after Rebecca has gone to bed (or has been driven there). He sits in the chair. The decanter empties at a rate that, week over week, would tell a surveillance accountant the precise quantum of his nightly intake — roughly six to nine ounces of bourbon at home, plus the flask-portions in the car, plus the morning maintenance dose. On bad nights he does not go to bed at all; he sleeps in the chair, in his clothes, and rises at 04:30 the next morning to begin again. On the worst nights he goes into the boy's room one more time, and stands there, and does not always leave the boy unharmed.

---

## II. THE WEEKEND

Weekends are more dangerous than weekdays. The lab's structure is gone. The decompression drive is gone. There is no occupational alibi for sobriety, however limited; he begins drinking earlier — by 10:00 on Saturday, sometimes earlier — and the day has no exit hour.

**Saturday morning** he may attempt a project: a household repair, a piece of yard work, a car-maintenance task. He has tools and he is competent with them. The projects are characteristically begun with concentration and abandoned in irritation, often left half-finished for weeks; the unfinished projects accumulate in the garage and the yard as a physical record of his unraveling executive function under unstructured time. Rebecca learns not to ask about them.

**Saturday afternoon** is often when he reads — technical material, the accumulated radiation-and-genetics file, occasionally fiction (he favors a kind of dour mid-century realism). He drinks steadily through the reading.

**Saturday evening** is the highest-risk window of the week. The accumulation of alcohol meets the accumulation of unstructured proximity to his wife and child. The household tactics for surviving Saturday evening — Rebecca taking the child out, to a park or to her mother's; the child put to bed unusually early; the television on as a buffer — are themselves part of the home's circadian defense.

**Sunday** is observably the quietest day. He nurses a hangover; he is sullen rather than violent; he reads the Sunday paper at length; he naps in the chair in the afternoon. The household exhales. Sunday evening tightens again as the workweek's anticipated structure begins to reassert pressure on him, and the late Sunday hour can produce a flare — a verbal demolition of Rebecca, less often a beating — that is functionally the discharge of his anticipatory dread of Monday morning's tremor.

He does not attend church. Rebecca, if she goes, goes alone or with Bruce. He does not socialize on weekends. There are no visitors to the house, save very occasional family — Rebecca's relations, whom he tolerates with thin courtesy and whose departures he counts the minutes to. He has, in the entire depicted span of the marriage, no friend who crosses the threshold.

---

## III. HABITS OF PLACE

He owns the chair in the living room. No one else sits in it. The wear pattern on the upholstery, the cigarette-burn marks on the side table beside it, the ring stains from the bourbon glass, the angle at which the floor lamp is tilted toward the reading material on his lap — these would be archaeologically legible in any post-mortem inventory of the home.

He owns the head of the kitchen table.

He owns the bathroom in the mornings.

He owns the garage, where the unfinished projects live and where, in the canonical record, he keeps certain tools and certain bottles separate from those in the house — a secondary cache, the existence of which Rebecca knows about and the contents of which she does not catalogue.

He does *not* enter the child's room except to enforce or to loom. The child's room is, by negative space, Rebecca's territory, and the violation of it is one of his most charged behaviors.

The bedroom he shares with Rebecca is, by the period preceding the killing, effectively a contested zone in which the dominant interpersonal mode is avoidance: she goes to bed before him on most nights; he comes in late and either passes out or initiates a confrontation. Sexual contact in the marriage, after the pregnancy, is depicted as sparse, coercive when it occurs, and an additional vector of his contamination-anxiety (the act both proves and reproduces the curse).

---

## IV. HABITS OF TIME

He is, as noted, punctual to the minute at work. He is punctual to dinner. He is punctual to the threshold call.

His sense of time degrades steeply after the third drink. Surveillance video of the late evening would record him losing the thread of whatever he is reading, the page held open without progress for ten or fifteen minutes; would record him beginning a sentence to Rebecca and not finishing it; would record him standing in a room for a long moment without apparent purpose, then sitting back down.

Anniversaries he does not mark. Rebecca's birthday: forgotten or grudgingly acknowledged with a perfunctory gift. Bruce's birthday: actively negated — the canonical record includes scenes in which Brian's response to celebration of the child is to spoil it, to ruin the cake, to extinguish the candles in a fit. Their wedding anniversary: marked once or twice early, never thereafter.

Calendar holidays produce above-baseline violence. Christmas is bad. New Year's is worse. The forced sociability of family holidays meets the unstructured time of weekends and the inflated drinking of seasonal "permission."

---

## V. TICS, TELLS, AND MICRO-BEHAVIORS

Several behaviors recur across the canonical depictions with enough frequency to be considered stable signatures, and any week of surveillance would log them repeatedly:

- **The jaw-tighten** at the sound of the child's voice, particularly the child's laughter. Observable; involuntary; precedes nearly all of his hostile acts toward the boy.
- **The two-finger drumming** on the side of his glass when listening to Rebecca speak — a metronome of held-back rebuttal.
- **The pause-before-naming** — when he refers to the child to a third party (a colleague, a relative) he hesitates a fraction before saying "Bruce," and frequently says "the boy" or "the child" instead. He almost never uses the diminutive "Brucie" that Rebecca uses; when he does, it is mocking.
- **The cleaning of the glasses.** He wears reading glasses; he cleans them constantly, particularly when stressed; the act is a tic of preparation, often immediately preceding a verbal or physical attack.
- **The single deep exhale through the nose** that precedes him standing up from the chair to initiate violence. Rebecca, in the canonical record, has been depicted as recognizing this breath; the child, even pre-verbally, has been depicted flinching at it.
- **The shoe-shine ritual** in the small hours after a particularly bad night — a compulsive restoration of an immaculate surface.
- **The radio dial.** He retunes constantly; he cannot let a station settle; this is a frustration-tolerance failure rendered in furniture.
- **The phrase architecture.** His sentences to Rebecca, when contemptuous, follow a stable grammar: a flat declarative, a pause, an interrogative tag that is not a question — "You think that's clever. Do you." He has perhaps a dozen such constructions and recycles them.
- **The look at Bruce.** Described above. Not a glare; an appraisal. The most-noted single behavior in the entire canonical record of him.

---

## VI. A WEEK OF SURVEILLANCE: COMPOSITE LOG

What an honest week's intercept would produce:

- **Five weekday mornings** of identical bathroom-and-departure choreography, with the maintenance-dose audible only because the tap mask is imperfect.
- **Five commutes** routed identically outbound and variably extended inbound, with cigarette and flask use logged on the return.
- **Five workdays** of competent, unremarkable institutional behavior, no after-hours socializing, no observable substance use on premises beyond the locked-drawer flask sipped perhaps twice weekly behind a closed door.
