- **Five evenings** of phased domestic compression — perimeter check, controlled drink, dinner, disinhibition, unsafe hour — with violence (verbal or physical, against Rebecca or against the child) occurring on perhaps three of the five and intensifying as the week progresses.
- **One Saturday** of an abandoned project, daylong drinking, and a high-probability flare in the evening.
- **One Sunday** of hungover quiet bracketed by morning sullenness and an anticipatory Sunday-night discharge.
- **Zero visitors. Zero friends. Zero unscheduled outings. Zero calls of intimacy received or made. Zero church. Zero hobby. Zero recorded act of physical tenderness toward wife or child.**

The week is a closed loop. It does not include the variables a life ordinarily includes — surprise, generosity, play, repair. It includes only the maintenance of the public mask, the discharge of the private rage, and the substance-mediated traverse between them. It is a life organized around the prevention of one perception (that he has become his father) by the continuous enactment of the thing that perception would name.

A surveillance officer compiling the file at the end of the week would, if competent, write the same sentence three different agencies' analysts have written about three different men in three different decades, and it would be exactly correct here: *the household is the crime scene; the schedule is the weapon; the only question is when.*


## Relationships I — Family & Origin Bonds

# RELATIONSHIPS I — FAMILY & ORIGIN BONDS

*UATU Dossier on Brian Banner — Section 6*

The family field around Brian Banner is small, sealed, and lethal. It is not a network; it is a closed circuit, four nodes (father, mother of record, wife, son), with the current running one direction — downhill, from the abuser of one generation to the abused of the next, terminating in two deaths and one fractured psyche that will require multiple Hulks to contain. What follows maps each bond at the granularity of a long-term surveillance file: history, daily dynamics, evolution, the trust/safety contour, the failure mode (i.e. the exact mechanism by which the bond goes lethal), and the leverage each party held — or could have held — over him.

---

## 6.1 — THE FATHER ("Old Man Banner")

**Status in the record.** Unnamed in published canon. Appears chiefly in the dream-therapy sequence Doc Samson walks Bruce through in *Incredible Hulk* #377, and in fragmentary flashbacks Brian himself surfaces (in drunken monologue) during the #312 sequence. He is the off-stage prime mover of the entire case.

**History.** Brian was beaten as a child — chronically, by his father, in a home that the canon depicts as cold, rural-coded, religiously inflected without being doctrinally specified, and structured around the father's authority as an absolute. The beatings are referred to as routine rather than episodic; the implication is a household in which the child's body was the regulator of the adult's affect. There is no depicted maternal protector on the paternal side; Brian's mother (Bruce's paternal grandmother) is essentially absent from the record, which is itself diagnostic — either she could not or would not intervene, and either way the boy learned that no adult would interpose between him and the fist. The household appears to have been economically and socially functional on its surface (Brian emerges from it credentialed enough to become a physicist), which means the violence was, like Brian's own later violence, sequestered to the domestic theater. He learned the compartment from the man who built it.

**Dynamics.** The relationship as Brian carried it forward was structured around three transmitted contents: (1) that love and violence are not opposites but the same operation under different lighting; (2) that the father's word is reality, and the child's perception of pain is a lie to be corrected; (3) that masculinity is the maintenance of dominance under threat of dissolution. Brian's later contamination delusion is best understood as a metaphysical rewriting of this last content — he experienced his father as having *put something into him* that ruined him, and he transposed this experience, in adulthood, from the moral register (I was ruined by being beaten) to the somatic register (I was ruined by radiation, in my germ-line). The displacement preserves the affect (I am contaminated by my father) while moving the cause to a domain where Brian, the rationalist, has explanatory authority. It is an elegant defense and a catastrophic one.

**Evolution.** The bond does not evolve; it ossifies. Brian never confronts his father in the record, never reconciles, never repudiates. The father is, by the time of the published flashbacks, either dead or absent — Brian never visits, never receives visits. The father lives on entirely as an introject: the voice in Brian's head that calls him weak when he is tender, that calls him soft when he hesitates to strike. When Brian beats Bruce, the file should read this as a three-generation event in a single act: the grandfather's hand moving through the father's arm onto the grandson's body.

**Trust / safety.** Zero, retrospectively. The defining lesson of the bond is that the largest adult in a room is the source of danger. Brian carried this lesson into every subsequent relationship as both victim's intuition and aggressor's playbook — he knew exactly where to hit because he had been hit there.

**Failure mode.** The bond failed at origin and was never repaired; the failure is total and is the substrate of everything else in this file. The specific mechanism: the father converted the son's interior into uninhabitable territory, and the son spent the rest of his life trying to evict the tenant by installing him in someone else.

**Leverage.** The father's leverage over Brian was absolute and posthumous. Even after death (presumed), the father commanded Brian's behavior through the introject — Brian could not act against his father's template because to do so would require acknowledging that the template was a wound rather than a nature. Inverse leverage — what Brian had over his father — was nil. He never sought it. The one act of inverse leverage available to a son of such a father, refusing to reproduce the pattern, was the act Brian was constitutionally incapable of performing.

---

## 6.2 — THE MOTHER (paternal, Brian's mother)

**Status in the record.** Effectively unrendered. A negative space.

**History.** No depicted intervention in the beatings. No depicted nurturance toward Brian as a child that he carries forward as a protected memory. (Contrast this sharply with Bruce, who carries Rebecca's tenderness as the one preserved good object of his childhood — Brian appears to have no equivalent.) Whether she was a fellow victim, a colluder, or a depressed bystander cannot be determined from the canon, but the *behavioral consequence* in Brian is identical regardless of which: the maternal function did not arrive when needed, and Brian grew up with no internalized template for a woman who could or would protect a child from a man.

**Dynamics.** The crucial dynamic is the absence of one. Brian's adult inability to tolerate Rebecca's maternal warmth toward Bruce is over-determined, but one substantial determinant is this: he never had it himself, and the sight of his son receiving what he was denied is a narcissistic injury of the most archaic kind. Envy, in the Kleinian sense — the impulse to destroy the good thing precisely because it is good and not one's own — is the active mechanism. He cannot bear to watch Bruce be mothered, and so he severs the dyad, first by interruption, then by terror, finally by murder.

**Evolution.** None traceable. She does not appear in the adult Brian's life as a corresponding figure; he does not bring Rebecca to meet her, does not bring infant Bruce to her, does not speak of her. The bond was so insubstantial that it left no adult shadow except in the form of the missing template.

**Trust / safety.** Indeterminate but functionally zero, since the operational lesson is that women in the home will not protect children from men. This lesson is what Bruce will later, terribly, half-confirm in his own childhood — Rebecca tries and dies trying.

**Failure mode.** Failure by omission. The mother who does not interpose teaches the child that interposition is not a category of behavior the world contains. Brian carries this into adulthood as a flatness in the perception of women's protective capacity, which expresses on the aggressive side as contempt for Rebecca's attempts to shield Bruce.

**Leverage.** None retained.

---

## 6.3 — REBECCA BANNER (wife)

**Status.** Murdered by Brian, in a parking lot, in front of their six-year-old son, during an attempted departure. The most extensively rendered relationship in the file and the one in which Brian is most visible as a man, not merely a mechanism.

**History — formation.** The canon does not date the courtship precisely, but the inferable contour is this: Brian, a young scientist, presentable, articulate, with the credentialing intelligence of a research physicist and the compartmentalization that allowed his cruelty to be invisible to outsiders, met Rebecca in a context (university, social, possibly war-period or immediately post-) where she encountered the public Brian and not the private one. Rebecca, as depicted, was warm, intelligent, capable of deep maternal love, and — critically — drawn to a man who needed saving. The pattern of women who marry men with contamination-self-images is well established clinically: she would have perceived a wound and believed she could love it closed. She would have been the first person in Brian's life who looked at him with sustained tenderness. He would have experienced this, at first, as miraculous; then, as the structure of his self-image (I am contaminated) reasserted, as a category error she was making (she cannot really love me because I am not lovable, therefore she is either a fool or a liar or staging something). This is the engine of the marriage's degradation.

**Dynamics — the marriage as a daily system.**

- **The pre-Bruce period.** Detectably less violent in the record; Brian's contamination preoccupation was already active (the sterilization attempt predates the conception), but the household had not yet acquired its second target. Rebecca bore the full brunt during this phase. The depicted pattern is the classic battering cycle: tension build-up, acute incident, contrition or at least the cessation of attack, honeymoon-phase warmth (probably tied for Brian to drinking-down or to the temporary relief of having discharged the affect), tension build-up again. Cycle period likely measured in days to weeks. Rebecca learned to read his weather — barometric reading of an unsafe man is a specific cognitive skill, and she would have developed it to a high level. Bruce later inherits this skill as one of his foundational competences.
- **The pregnancy.** A catastrophe in Brian's interior. Rebecca conceived against his expressed wish (the sterilization had been incomplete or had been a procurement-of-means he had not yet executed — the canon is slightly ambiguous; the operative point is that conception occurred and Brian experienced it as proof that contamination had escaped containment). His behavior during pregnancy: surveillance of Rebecca's body, attempts to terminate the pregnancy or to coerce her toward termination (inferable from his post-natal hostility), withdrawal of whatever residual warmth he had retained. Rebecca chose the child over the husband at this juncture — silently, in the only way available to her, by carrying to term.
- **The Bruce period.** The household reconfigures around a triangle. Rebecca pours every protective and loving capacity she possesses into the infant. Brian reads this as her defection. The violence against Rebecca escalates and is now joined by violence against and around the child. Rebecca's daily life becomes a triage operation: position her body between Brian and Bruce, absorb the blow that would otherwise land on the boy, redirect Brian's attention onto herself when she perceives Bruce in his crosshairs. She is, in the technical sense, a human shield, and she performs this function for years, until the day she attempts to convert the strategy into exit.
- **Sexual register.** Not depicted, properly invisible to the file, but inferable as coercive within marriage and as a further site of the contamination-anxiety (intercourse as the route through which the contamination becomes child). Brian's sexuality is likely organized around the same domination-as-affect-regulation pattern as the rest of his behavior.
- **Public-facing register.** They presented as a family. Neighbors, colleagues, extended kin do not appear to have intervened, which means either Rebecca concealed effectively (likely — battered women become expert concealers, both from shame and from the knowledge that intervention often increases danger) or the surrounding social fabric was indifferent (also likely, given the period). Brian's compartmentalization held. He did not hit her at the lab Christmas party.

**Evolution.** From idealization (her tenderness as miracle) through possession (her tenderness as his property) through devaluation (her tenderness as defection, once redirected to Bruce) to objectification (her body as obstacle between him and the monster-child) to elimination (her body removed from the obstacle position by being killed). This is a textbook trajectory and Brian executes it without deviation.

**Trust / safety.** Asymmetric in the extreme. Rebecca was the only person Brian trusted enough to be cruel in front of without consequence — a perverse form of trust, but real. She knew his interior in a way no colleague did; she had seen him drunk, frightened, weeping, raging, and small. He could not tolerate having been seen, and the killing is partly a killing of the witness. From Rebecca's side, trust collapsed in stages: trust in his promises (early), trust in his capacity to change (middle), trust in his containment (late) — and the final collapse, trust that he would not kill her, is the one that prompts the departure that prompts the killing. Her safety with him was always conditional and was finally revoked at the worst possible moment, which is the moment battered partners are most often killed.

**Failure mode.** Uxoricide at the moment of attempted exit. The mechanism: Rebecca's departure registered for Brian as the realization of his core terror (abandonment by the woman who had impossibly loved the contaminated thing he was), as a betrayal coalition with the monster-child (she was leaving *with* Bruce, not merely leaving), and as a loss of the only theater in which he could maintain dominance. The three terrors converged in a parking lot and he killed her with his hands, in public enough to be witnessed, in front of the child whose existence was the proximate trigger. This is not a loss-of-control killing — it is the predictable terminal phase of an unmanaged battering relationship in which the perpetrator's psychic structure cannot survive the partner's departure.

**Leverage.** Rebecca's leverage over Brian, properly understood, was enormous and unusable. She held the only mirror he could not look away from — her continuing love was the only evidence that contradicted his contamination self-image, and as such it was both the thing that could have saved him and the thing he most needed to destroy. Had she been able to deploy that leverage — to compel him into treatment under threat of departure early enough, with sufficient external scaffolding — there is a thin clinical possibility he could have been moved. The window closed long before the parking lot. By the end her only remaining leverage was the leverage of exit, which is precisely the leverage that triggers lethal escalation in this typology. She used the only tool she had left, and it killed her.

Brian's leverage over Rebecca was the standard battering portfolio: fear, isolation, financial dependence (he held the income), social shame, the threat to Bruce, and the long erosion of her conviction that any other life was available to her. She broke that leverage on the day she tried to leave. The breaking cost her her life and gave her son the one act of his mother he would carry forward as proof that someone had tried.

---

## 6.4 — ROBERT BRUCE BANNER (son)

**Status.** Alive. The eventual agent (involuntary, repressed) of Brian's death. The bearer of the entire downstream legacy. The relationship in which Brian's pathology achieves its most complete and most catastrophic expression.

**History — pre-natal.** The relationship begins before birth, in the most operative sense, with Brian's pre-conception conviction that any child of his would be a monster. Bruce is, in his father's mind, *already* monstrous before he is *yet* anything. This is the foundational fact of the entire dynamic. The child does not arrive into a neutral parental field and earn his treatment; he arrives into a pre-formed delusional template, and every subsequent observation his father makes of him is filtered through the template.

**History — infancy.** Brian's affect toward the infant was, per the record, revulsion-dominant from the first sight. He could not hold the baby without flinching; he avoided night-feeds (this is inferable, not depicted, but consistent); he resented every milestone Rebecca celebrated. The infant's normal behaviors — crying, sleeping, feeding — were re-encoded by Brian as evidence (the cry is "not a human cry"; the sleep is "too still"; the feeding is "too greedy"). The remarkable feature here is the absence of even intermittent positive engagement; most batterers of children show some bonding, some moments of pride or play, some photographic-evidence-grade good fatherhood. Brian shows almost none. The child was conscripted from arrival as the container for the disowned self, and containers do not get held with tenderness.

**History — toddlerhood and early childhood.** Bruce, as the record makes clear, was extraordinarily bright early — reading young, doing mathematics young, showing precisely the intellectual gifts his father had and more. This was the worst possible developmental profile for survival in this household. Each demonstration of intelligence produced in Brian a triple injury: (a) confirmation of the "not-human" delusion (this is not a normal child, therefore my fear was right), (b) narcissistic wounding (this child will surpass me, his is mine and not mine, he is taking what is mine), and (c) envy of the intellect itself, which Brian had bought at the price of his soul and which Bruce was getting for free. Bruce learned, very early, to dim — to perform competence below his actual capacity in his father's presence, to fail strategically at the dinner table, to hide books. This is one of the foundational adaptive skills he carries into adulthood: the concealment of his own power from a watching authority. The Hulk will later be the thing he could not, finally, conceal.

**History — middle childhood, to age six.** The household stabilized into the configuration that ended in Rebecca's death: Brian drinking, raging, beating; Rebecca interposing; Bruce, the child, developing the specific cognitive style of a battered child in a battering household — hypervigilant scanning of the father's affect, micro-reading of footsteps and door-handles and breath patterns, a startle threshold so low it functions as a constant background state, dissociation as the only remaining defense once physical escape is foreclosed. The dissociative substrate that will later, under gamma trauma, organize into multiple personalities is laid down here, in this house, by this man. The Savage Hulk is, in part, the rage Bruce could not express at his father, stored and pressurized until a radiological accident gave it a body.

**The killing of Rebecca.** Bruce witnessed it. He was six. Brian then coached and threatened him into silence — the specifics inferable from the legal outcome (the child witness was suppressed). The coaching would have included: it was an accident, you didn't see what you saw, if you say anything they will take you away to a worse place, no one will believe you, this is our family secret. Bruce internalized all of it. The repression that follows is one of the great repressions in the published canon; it requires Doc Samson, decades later and under extreme intervention, to dredge it back up.

**History — Brian's institutionalization.** Bruce was removed from the household, raised by extended kin (the canon places him with an aunt — Aunt Susan, in some renderings — during this period). The relationship with Brian goes into abeyance. Bruce grows up, goes to school, distinguishes himself, builds the public scientist self that mirrors his father's in form but is its inverse in content — Bruce's intellect is directed toward *not* being his father, toward control of dangerous forces rather than discharge of them.

**The reunion and the second killing.** Brian, released from institutionalization, finds the adult Bruce. The encounter is not reconciliatory; Brian initiates it to reassert the old order. The confrontation moves to Rebecca's grave (Brian's choice of venue; he stages the encounter at the site of his greatest crime, which is either an unconscious confession or a deliberate provocation, and is plausibly both). Brian physically attacks. Bruce, no longer six, defends. Brian's head strikes the headstone. Brian dies. Bruce represses the killing on the spot — the repression itself a measure of how completely his father's voice still controlled the interior, since the voice could not tolerate the data point "I killed my father" any more than the six-year-old could tolerate "I watched him kill my mother."

**Posthumous relationship.** This is, in some ways, the most active phase of the bond. Brian dies but does not depart. He installs himself in Bruce's interior as the Guilt Hulk's lineage of internal persecutors, as the jeering voice that re-emerges in dissociative episodes, as the metaphysical figure that *Immortal Hulk* later treats as the originating wound of the green door. Bruce's adult psychic life is, structurally, an ongoing relationship with a dead father who refuses to be dead. Every Hulk identity is a different attempt to organize a response to him: the Savage Hulk fights him directly, the Joe Fixit outmaneuvers him, the Professor reasons against him, the Devil/Immortal Hulk metabolizes him into a worldview. None succeed in evicting him.

**Trust / safety.** From Bruce's side toward Brian: never. The infant never had safe arms here; the child learned the house was a hunting ground; the adult discovered, in the gravesite confrontation, that his father had not changed; the post-mortem son discovered that his father had not even fully left. From Brian's side toward Bruce: a perverse anti-trust — he trusted Bruce to remain the container for the disowned self, trusted that the child would absorb the projection and not break under it, and was correct in the technical sense (Bruce never named the projection to Brian's face, never refused it; it took the gamma bomb to externalize the absorbed material into a separate body).

**Failure mode.** Total. The bond fails at every juncture a father-son bond can fail: at gestation (Brian wants the child not to exist), at birth (Brian recoils), at infancy (no bonding), at toddlerhood (envy and projection), at school age (concealed intelligence as survival strategy), at the witnessing of the matricide (the child is coerced into colluding with the father against his own perception), at the institutionalization (no contact, no repair), at the reunion (renewed assault), at the death (no mourning is possible because the death is repressed), at the post-mortem (the introject continues the abuse internally for decades). The failure has the perverse completeness of a designed thing.

**Leverage.** Bruce's leverage over Brian, in life, was the leverage of a victim — that is, very nearly none, but with one specific terrible item: his existence itself was the standing refutation of Brian's wish to have been sterile. As long as Bruce lived, Brian's contamination delusion had its evidence, which both confirmed Brian's worldview (and was therefore desired) and tormented him (and was therefore intolerable). This is the double-bind that produced the cruelty: Bruce had to exist as proof and had to be punished for proving it. The child could not be killed (legally, socially, perhaps even by Brian's interior limits) but could be tormented endlessly, and was.

Brian's leverage over Bruce, in life, was near-total: the leverage of a parent over a small body in a closed house, plus the cognitive leverage of installing his voice inside the child's head so that the leverage outlasted physical proximity. In death, his leverage *increased*, because the introject could not be argued with, could not be left, could not be killed. The whole subsequent project of Bruce Banner's life — the gamma work, the multiple identities, the lifelong attempt to integrate — can be read, at one level, as the long campaign to revoke the leverage of a dead man. The campaign is, at the time of this dossier, still in progress.

---

## 6.5 — THE FAMILY SYSTEM AS A SINGLE OBJECT

A final note on the four-node system as such, since the family is a unit and not merely a sum.

The Banner household under Brian was a sealed thermodynamic system with one heat source (Brian's unmetabolized rage, fed by his own father's beatings and his contamination delusion), two heat sinks (Rebecca and Bruce), and no outlet. Such systems run hotter over time. Rebecca's death was the predictable rupture of the smaller sink; Brian's own death was the rupture of the source itself, achieved by the only remaining node in the system finally returning the energy that had been dumped into him. The system did not produce a survivor in the ordinary sense — Bruce walked out of it carrying every joule of unprocessed heat his father had ever generated, and the subsequent six decades of published canon are the record of that heat seeking a form it can rest in.

For surveillance purposes, the operative finding is this: Brian Banner is not adequately understood as an individual subject. He is the middle node of a three-generation transmission line. The grandfather is the source, Brian is


## Relationships II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

# RELATIONSHIPS II — Allies, Chosen Family & Mentors

## Prefatory Note on a Near-Empty Ledger

The standard dossier section under this heading typically runs long: a subject's adult life accretes confidants, work-friends, sponsors, mentors who shaped them, proteges they shaped in turn, the godparents at their child's christening, the colleague who covers for them when they are hungover, the AA sponsor, the priest, the brother-in-law they actually like. In Brian Banner's case the file is conspicuous for what is absent. Across roughly four decades of adult biography in the published record, the subject produces **no durable friendship, no chosen-family bond, no mentor relationship of substance, and no protege**. This is itself a finding of the first order. A man does not arrive at age forty with a wife he beats, a son he hates, and no allies anywhere by accident; the absence is a structural feature of the personality, not a sampling artifact of the comics page.

What follows, therefore, is not a roster of close relationships but a granular map of the *thin and instrumental* affiliative ties Brian did sustain — the para-relationships, the functional acquaintances, the institutional attachments that stood in for human ones — together with the depicted or strongly inferable individuals who functioned in adjacent roles (the doctoral advisor, the colleague-of-record, the bartender, the institutional clinician, the parish-priest-equivalent). Each is treated at the depth the file format demands, with the caveat that depth here often means cataloguing the *limits* of the bond rather than its richness.

---

## 1. Rebecca Banner (née Walters) — The Misfiled Ally

Rebecca belongs more properly in the spouse section, and is treated there at length, but she must be addressed here for a specific reason: in the architecture of Brian's interior life, **Rebecca was the only person he ever permitted into the category of "ally," and her demotion from that category — her recategorization as enemy, as conspirator with the monster-child — is the load-bearing event of his adult psychology**.

The courtship-era Rebecca, reconstructable from the few warm fragments in #312 and the dream-recovered material of #377, functioned for Brian as a chosen-family-of-one: the first person in his life since his own mother (also abused, also unable to protect him) who offered uncomplicated tenderness. The trust extended in that window was real. He told her, on the evidence, at least *something* of his contamination fears — the sterilization attempt is depicted in the texts as known to her, which means it was confessed or discovered, either of which implies a degree of intimate access no other person in his life ever obtained.

**Failure mode:** the bond could not survive the arrival of a third person who displaced him in her attention. Rebecca's bonding with the infant Bruce was experienced by Brian not as an expansion of the family circle but as a defection. The ally became, in his perceptual world, a co-conspirator. From roughly Bruce's infancy onward Brian had **no allies of any kind left in his life** — the only one had switched sides.

**Leverage:** posthumously, total. Rebecca's memory is the one thing Brian could neither beat nor outdrink. The graveside confrontation that ends his life is staged at *her* monument; he comes back, on some level, to argue with her, and dies there.

---

## 2. The Father (Name Withheld in Canon) — Anti-Mentor and Permanent Internal Object

Brian's father is referenced rather than named in the texts (Mantlo #312; PAD #377 dream sequence). He is dead by the time Brian is an adult, but is the single most influential figure in Brian's adult life — more present than Rebecca, more present than any colleague. He belongs in this section under the inverted category of **anti-mentor**: the relationship that modeled adulthood, masculinity, fatherhood, and the management of a household for Brian, and which he reproduced with grim fidelity.

**History as reconstructable.** A drinker. A beater of his wife and of Brian. The texts give us flash-images: a belt, a kitchen, a child cowering. There is no depicted episode of paternal warmth. The mother, glimpsed, is a battered figure who does not intervene effectively. This is the entire surviving record and it is enough.

**Dynamics during Brian's childhood.** Hypervigilance to the father's moods, learned in the body; the somatic encoding of an authority figure as imminent threat. Brian almost certainly developed the standard battered-child repertoire: scanning footsteps for cadence, scanning breath for liquor, freezing rather than fleeing, attempts at appeasement that failed unpredictably. These habits become the substrate for his adult cognitive style: domestic vigilance, alcohol-cue sensitivity (he can smell it on himself and on others), and a default assumption that intimacy is a venue for harm.

**Evolution.** The father dies before the texts open and is never the object of overt mourning. Brian's relationship to him goes underground and operates as introject: the internal voice that tells Brian he is contaminated, that the child is a monster, that a man's house is his domain. Every act of cruelty Brian commits is, at the structural level, a transaction with this internal figure — a demonstration of having become him, which is the only resolution Brian can imagine for the wound of having been beaten by him.

**Trust/safety:** zero in life; the only "safety" available was correct prediction of when blows would fall.

**Failure mode:** Brian's attempt to *break* the lineage (the sterilization) failed because the identification was already complete — he tried to prevent a child while already being the father who would beat one. The negation was a confession.

**Leverage:** absolute and lifelong. The dead father is the figure to whom every other relationship in Brian's life is, at some level, a footnote.

---

## 3. The Doctoral Advisor / Senior Scientific Patron (Inferred)

No mentor is named in canon, but Brian's trajectory — doctorate, security clearance, employment at restricted nuclear research facilities in the postwar / early Cold War era — requires the existence of at least one senior figure who vouched for him, signed his thesis, wrote his clearance recommendation, and slotted him into a first position. The total-surveillance file would name this person; in the absence of nomination, the role and its dynamics can still be reconstructed.

**History.** Postwar American physics was a small world. Brian's advisor would have been someone shaped by the Manhattan generation — possibly a junior figure on that project, more likely a student of one. The relationship would have begun in Brian's mid-twenties, continued through dissertation defense, and persisted as a professional reference relationship into Brian's early career.

**Dynamics.** With authority Brian is — per the reaction-formation pattern documented in §3 — presentable, deferential, competent, intellectually adequate to the work. He would have been a satisfactory student: not brilliant (his envy of Bruce's young intellect tells us Brian knew himself to be merely capable, not exceptional), but reliable, hard-working, and crucially *contained* in professional settings. An advisor would have experienced him as a slightly remote, slightly humorless graduate student who delivered on time and did not socialize much. There would have been no invitations home for dinner; if there were, they would have been refused or attended once and not repeated, because Rebecca-and-Brian as a social unit could not survive sustained outside observation.

**Evolution.** Likely deterioration after Brian's institutionalization. A patron who had written his clearance letters would have been professionally embarrassed by the murder-and-commitment and would have quietly dropped him. There is no evidence in the texts of any professional re-entry to physics after Brian's release; he disappears from the scientific record into the figure who shows up at the gravesite. The career, in other words, ends with the marriage, and the mentor relationship ends with the career.

**Trust/safety:** moderate at the professional surface, zero at any deeper layer. Brian would never have disclosed the home situation to this person.

**Failure mode:** the mentor was a *role* relationship, not a person relationship; when Brian ceased to occupy the role of promising junior scientist, the bond evaporated, because there had been no bond, only a role-to-role contract.

**Leverage:** the mentor had professional leverage over Brian (recommendations, access) that Brian managed by performing well within strict boundaries. Brian had no leverage over the mentor and sought none.

---

## 4. The Colleague-of-Record at the Research Facility (Inferred Composite)

Every man who holds a research post for a decade-plus has a *work friend* — the person across the corridor, the one he eats lunch with on Tuesdays, the one who covers his desk when he is out. For Brian this person existed and was, by every structural indication, **a single-channel acquaintance with whom the bond was bounded entirely by the workplace and by topics adjacent to the work**.

**History.** Met at hire or shortly after. Shared a building, a coffee pot, a security briefing cadence. Probably one or two people, not a circle — Brian's interpersonal bandwidth does not support a circle.

**Dynamics.** Conversation domains: calculation, equipment, departmental politics, sports possibly, weather. Domains avoided: family, drinking, anything that required Brian to render an interior. Brian's presentation in this register is reconstructable from the compartmentalization documented elsewhere: dry, competent, slightly sardonic, capable of a joke but not a confidence. He would have been known as a *private* man, which in a 1950s–60s American workplace was unremarkable enough to require no further inquiry. The colleague would have noticed: the smell of liquor on some mornings; the occasional bruised knuckle (explained as a household accident, accepted); a wife glimpsed at a Christmas function looking thin and quiet; a child, when mentioned at all, mentioned with a tightness that the colleague filed under *some men just don't talk about their kids*.

**Evolution.** The colleague is the person who, after Rebecca's death and the institutional commitment, says to other colleagues some version of *I never would have guessed* — and means it, because the compartmentalization had been that successful. He does not visit Brian in the institution. He sends, perhaps, a card. The relationship ends because it was a workplace contract and Brian has left the workplace.

**Trust/safety:** functional. Brian could trust this person to do his job, to not pry, to not gossip beyond ordinary office gossip. He could not trust him with anything real and never tested it.

**Failure mode:** the relationship was a thin film; it tore at the first stressor (the arrest) and there was no substrate beneath.

**Leverage:** Brian had a small reserve of professional goodwill here that he never cashed in. The colleague had, in retrospect, more information about Brian than Brian realized — the bruised knuckles, the morning liquor — but did not act on it, both because the era did not, and because there was no relational invitation to do so.

---

## 5. The Bartender — The Closest Thing to a Confessor

In a file of this kind the bartender is rarely incidental. For a man of Brian's drinking pattern — daily, sustained, with disinhibition episodes — there was almost certainly a regular establishment, probably more than one across the years, and almost certainly a tender who knew his order, his cadence, and a censored version of his complaints.

**History.** A working physicist in a small research-town context would have had limited venues. Brian's drinking is depicted as both at-home and elsewhere; the *elsewhere* implies a bar or two within a short drive of work or home, frequented after shift, possibly the kind of place that catered to lab personnel.

**Dynamics.** Brian at a bar would have been a quiet drinker for the first two, more talkative by the third, and grievance-prone by the fourth. The bartender would have heard, in fragments across years: that his wife did not understand him; that his son was *not right*; that he, Brian, knew things about radiation that others did not; that his father had been a son of a bitch. The fragments would not have cohered for the bartender into the picture they cohere into here, because bartenders hear such fragments from many men. But the bartender heard *more* of Brian's interior than the colleague, the mentor, or arguably anyone other than Rebecca, because alcohol lowered the compartmentalization wall enough to let language out.

**Evolution.** Brian would have changed bars periodically — the standard alcoholic rotation when one venue's staff begins to read him too clearly or when an incident (a refused last drink, a near-altercation) makes return uncomfortable. There is no single bartender across the whole arc; there is a *role* occupied serially.

**Trust/safety:** the lowest-stakes confidence in the file. Bartender hears it, bartender forgets it, bartender does not appear in court. Brian could speak there with a freedom he could not exercise anywhere else, precisely because the listener had no entry into the rest of his life.

**Failure mode:** the bond is structurally unable to escalate into help. The bartender does not call a hotline, does not telephone Rebecca, does not flag the man at the end of the bar talking about his monster-son. The relationship is a pressure-release valve, not an intervention.

**Leverage:** none in either direction. This is the relationship's appeal to Brian.

---

## 6. The Institutional Clinician (Post-Commitment)

After Rebecca's death and the commitment, Brian was under the care of at least one psychiatrist of record at the receiving facility, and probably under serial care across a period of years. The clinician is unnamed in canon but the role is canonically required. This is the only adult relationship in Brian's life in which a counterparty was professionally obligated to understand him, and it is therefore worth careful treatment.

**History.** Intake would have included the homicide charge, the acquittal-by-insufficient-evidence (the child witness suppressed), and the commitment order in lieu of incarceration. The clinician would have inherited Brian as a high-profile case: educated patient, articulate, capable of forming a working alliance on the surface, with a homicide and a wife in the file.

**Dynamics.** Brian's behavior in clinical settings would have been a more elaborate version of his behavior with superiors — presentable, articulate, capable of producing the language of insight without the substance of it. He would have spoken plausibly about his father, his drinking, his "stress." He would not have spoken truthfully about the contamination delusion or the projective hatred of the child — these were the load-bearing structures and were therefore the most defended. He would have learned, quickly, what kind of language got him toward release and produced it.

The clinician would have struggled with him. The diagnostic picture is genuinely difficult — encapsulated delusional content, intact reality-testing in most domains, secondary antisocial features overlaid on a chassis of complex developmental trauma. A clinician of the period (1960s–70s, depending on chronology) would have lacked the framework to name half of this and would have settled for a working label and a program of medication and milieu. Brian would have served his time, attended his groups, and exited.

**Evolution.** The clinician relationship ends at discharge. There is no depicted outpatient follow-through. Brian re-emerges into Bruce's adolescence/young adulthood with the contamination structure entirely intact and the only modification being a thin behavioral varnish learned in the ward.

**Trust/safety:** asymmetric and instrumental. Brian trusted the clinician to be a gatekeeper whose decisions could be manipulated. He did not trust the clinician with anything real, and the clinician's failure to extract the real material was not a clinical failure so much as the predictable outcome of a defended patient with literally homicidal stakes in concealment.

**Failure mode:** the entire treatment misses the encapsulated delusion. Brian leaves with the same psyche he came in with and proceeds to act on it within years.

**Leverage:** the clinician had administrative leverage (release/hold). Brian had cognitive leverage (he was articulate enough to manage the encounter). The clinician was not, in any meaningful sense, a mentor or ally — but is the closest figure in the file to one who *might have been* an intervention, had circumstances been different.

---

## 7. The Brother (Morris Walters' Side / Rebecca's Family) — Failed Conscript Allies

Rebecca had family — most notably her brother Morris Walters (uncle to Bruce, father to Jennifer Walters / She-Hulk), established in the broader canon. The Walters family represents Brian's only *available* chosen-family pool — the in-laws who, in any ordinary marriage, would have been Sunday dinners and shared holidays.

**History.** The texts depict the Walters family at distance from the Banner household, particularly during Bruce's childhood. Brian's wife had a brother who became a sheriff, a niece who became a lawyer; this is a family with civic spine, the kind of family that asks questions if a sister shows up bruised.

**Dynamics.** Brian's strategy with the in-laws would have been classic batterer-isolation: minimize contact, control the venue when contact was unavoidable, demean Rebecca's family to her in private, produce friction that made visits costly, relocate if necessary. The geographic and emotional distance between the Banners and the Walters during Bruce's childhood is, on this reading, *engineered*, not incidental. Brian could not afford a brother-in-law with a badge taking an interest.

**Evolution.** After Rebecca's death the relationship goes from distant-and-managed to actively hostile. The Walters side knows, or strongly suspects, what happened. They are not in court for Brian during the proceedings except as a counterforce. Bruce, in the aftermath, is not — significantly — taken in by the Walters family as a young child; he ends up shuttled through other arrangements before later being close to Morris and Jennifer in adolescence. The reasons for this are dense and partly external, but Brian's living presence after his release would have been a deterrent to the Walters family taking custody of the boy.

**Trust/safety:** zero in both directions. The brother-in-law was, structurally, the most dangerous person in Brian's social field — the man with both the standing and the means to investigate him. Brian's management of this danger was avoidance and isolation of Rebecca.

**Failure mode:** the in-law relationship was never an ally relationship to fail; it was a threat to be neutralized, and Brian neutralized it well enough to keep operating until he didn't.

**Leverage:** the Walters side had latent leverage (familial standing to intervene) that Brian preempted by isolation. He had leverage in the form of control over Rebecca's movement, which is the leverage every isolator has.

---

## 8. The Son as Reluctant Late-Stage Approach to Reconciliation — and Why It Was Not One

A note is required here because the file format anticipates a section on "proteges" or "those the subject took under his wing," and the only candidate in Brian's entire biography is his own son, and the candidacy fails completely.

The post-institutional reappearance of Brian in Bruce's life — the period leading to the graveside confrontation — has the *surface* shape of a reconciliation attempt: an older man, released from a hospital, seeks out his adult son. In any other file this would be the late-life mentorship section. In Brian's it is not. The reappearance is not an offer of relationship; it is a resumption of dominance. He arrives not to know his son but to reassert the contamination narrative against him, and when met with the son's refusal he escalates to physical assault within the same encounter. There is no apology, no inquiry, no acknowledgment of Rebecca, no curiosity about who Bruce has become. The encounter is structurally identical to the encounters of Bruce's childhood, with the variable that the child is now an adult and the father is older and weaker. The collision with the headstone that ends Brian's life is the literal failure of that miscalibration: he attacked as if it were still 1955 in his kitchen, and the world had moved.

**This non-relationship is the closing entry in the allies-and-chosen-family ledger because it is the proof that the ledger was never going to be filled.** A man capable of one chosen bond produces, eventually, at least one. Brian, given a final chance with the only person on earth who still bore his name, produced violence instead. The category was empty because the capacity was missing.

---

## Summary of the Affiliative Map

The composite picture: a man with one genuine intimate bond (Rebecca), destroyed by him; one defining anti-mentor (the father), introjected and reproduced; a series of role-bounded professional acquaintances who knew a competent stranger; serial bartenders who heard a censored interior; an institutional clinician who was managed rather than engaged; in-laws kept at engineered distance; and no friends, no protege, no godparent, no confidant, no priest, no AA sponsor, no chess partner, no fishing buddy. The file's emptiness in this section is the single most efficient summary of the personality structure documented at length elsewhere in the dossier: **a man organized around the export of an internal monster onto the nearest available bearer cannot afford allies, because allies see, and seeing was the thing the entire defensive architecture was built to prevent**.


## Relationships III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

# RELATIONSHIPS III — Rivals, Enemies & Lovers

A note on scope before the file proper. Brian Banner's relational world was small and almost entirely interior. He had no friends of record, no documented mistresses, no recurring rivals in the conventional sense — no laboratory antagonist, no estranged brother, no neighborhood adversary the canon places at his door. What he had instead was a tight roster of figures, living and dead, against whom he organized himself; with each of them the relationship was either combat, dominion, or possession dressed as love. The category headings — Rivals, Enemies, Lovers — are therefore partly heuristic. With Brian, all three categories tend to collapse into a single one: people he could not bear, and could not leave alone.

What follows maps every individual in the record (and the small number whose presence is structurally certain even when underdrawn) at the level of granularity a longitudinal surveillance file would carry: how the relationship began, how it evolved, where the trust register sat at each phase, the recurrent failure mode, and the specific levers each party held or could have held over the other.

---

## 1. Rebecca Banner — Wife, Victim, Idealized-Then-Destroyed Object

### History

Courtship is undepicted in the canon and must be inferred. The Rebecca we see in flashback (Mantlo, *Hulk* #312; David, #s 376–377) is a woman of warmth, intelligence, and intact maternal capacity, married to a junior-to-mid-career physicist with prospects. The marriage predates Bruce; the conception of Bruce is the rupture point. Brian's behavior to her before the pregnancy is not extensively shown, but the structural reading — that her warmth is what he chose her for, and her warmth is what he later punished her for — is supported by the consistent flashback motif of Rebecca touching, comforting, and bonding with Bruce while Brian watches from a doorway or across a kitchen, drink in hand, face going still.

The trajectory is a textbook battering arc telescoped into a few years: initial period of relative containment; the sterilization episode and its failure to prevent conception; pregnancy as Brian's first sustained crisis; postpartum collapse of any remaining inhibition as he begins reading the infant Bruce as the predicted monster; chronic battery thereafter, escalating in frequency and severity; Rebecca's eventual decision to leave with the child; interception in the parking lot; her murder, in front of Bruce, by repeated blows to the head and body.

### Dynamics

Brian's relationship to Rebecca was organized around a primitive splitting between Rebecca-as-wife (his) and Rebecca-as-mother (Bruce's, and therefore disloyal). The two could not be integrated. When she behaved as wife — attentive to him, contained, deferential — he could tolerate her at the level of irritated possession. When she behaved as mother — when she lifted Bruce, soothed him, spoke to him in the cadence reserved for the loved child — Brian experienced it as collusion with the monster, and as theft of her from him. The cruelty was, in this sense, always also a demand: stop loving him, love only me, prove the child is what I say it is by refusing to defend him.

She did not comply. The record is consistent that Rebecca, within the constraints of a battered woman in a research-town professional household of that era, did what she could: she shielded Bruce when she could intercept the blow, she taught him to read early (one suspects partly as flight into the mind, partly because his gifts genuinely demanded it), she told him he was a good boy and that his father was wrong about him. Each of these acts was, from Brian's vantage, a fresh provocation.

### Evolution

- **Pre-conception:** containment. Verbal cruelty likely; physical violence possibly intermittent. The marriage is publicly intact.
- **Conception and pregnancy:** Brian's contamination delusion blooms. He has already attempted sterilization; the pregnancy is therefore experienced as fate breaking through his defense. Hostility toward Rebecca's body in this period is structurally certain.
- **Infancy of Bruce:** the dyad of mother and child becomes the persecutory object. Battery escalates. Brian's drinking pattern hardens around the evening hours when the household is most exposed.
- **Bruce's early childhood:** the household stabilizes into chronic terror. Rebecca's behavior is calibrated minute by minute to Brian's mood. She becomes an expert in his micro-tells (see Section 5: the empty-glass cue, the silence after the door, the particular flatness when his eyes fix on Bruce reading).
- **Decision to flee:** the most dangerous moment. The canon places the killing in a parking lot, which means she had gotten to a car, with Bruce, with at least some belongings. She had planned. He had detected. The interception is depicted as a sudden, public, unconcealed beating — the disinhibition is total.
- **Posthumously:** Rebecca becomes, in Brian's mind, retroactively guilty — the wife who tried to leave with the proof of his contamination, and who therefore confirmed his entire structure. He does not mourn her in any depicted register. The institutionalization that follows is fought as a legal/medical inconvenience, not undergone as grief.

### Trust / Safety

Zero safety, increasing over time toward lethal. Trust in the relational sense was unidirectional — Rebecca trusted that he would not kill her until, near the end, she correctly assessed that he would. Brian extended no trust in either direction; she was a possession to be monitored, not a partner to be relied on.

### Failure Mode

The relationship's failure mode is the most documented one in the spousal-homicide literature: a battering male with an encapsulated delusional system about his partner's loyalty, with severe alcohol use, with a history of escalating violence, kills at the moment of attempted separation. Brian conformed to this script in every particular. There was no idiosyncrasy in the killing; the only idiosyncrasy was the child witness and the legal slippage that followed.

### Leverage

What Rebecca *had* over him, and could not use: she was the only person whose continued presence in the house authenticated him as husband and father — the social roles that propped up his compensatory self. Her departure was therefore an existential threat, which is precisely why it triggered the homicide. She could not weaponize this leverage because doing so required her to survive long enough to.

What Brian had over her: physical superiority, financial control, the social authority of the credentialed scientist husband in a 1960s research community where her word against his would have been disbelieved, and — crucially — Bruce as hostage. The threat of what he would do to the child if she left alone, and what he had already done to her if she stayed, defined her decision space.

---

## 2. Robert Bruce Banner — Son, Designated Monster, Eventual Killer

### History

Bruce is, from the moment of his conception, not a son to Brian but a verdict. The pregnancy is the failure of the sterilization defense; the birth is the materialization of the contamination delusion; the infant is the monster the father has feared into being. There is no honeymoon period, no first-smile reprieve, no toddler charm that disarms him. The canon is unusually firm on this point: Brian's hostility to Bruce begins at birth and does not relent.

The relationship has three identifiable phases.

**Phase one — infancy through early childhood.** Brian is a hostile presence in the house, periodically violent toward Bruce directly, more often violent toward Rebecca in Bruce's sight, and continuously cold-running in his contempt. Bruce's precocity emerges in this period — early reading, early arithmetic, the cognitive over-functioning typical of children who have learned that the mind is the only escape from the room. Brian reads each milestone as evidence: a normal child does not do this; therefore this child is not normal; therefore the contamination is confirmed.

**Phase two — the murder and its aftermath, ages roughly five or six to early adolescence.** Bruce witnesses Rebecca's killing. Brian, in the immediate aftermath, coaches and threatens him into silence — the canon shows this as a deliberate, sober-enough act of intimidation by the father, not a drunken outburst. The legal proceedings end with Brian institutionalized rather than imprisoned, the child witness suppressed by some combination of his age, the family's standing, and his own dissociative shutdown. Bruce is placed (per various tellings) with an aunt — Susan Drake / Aunt Susan — and Brian disappears from his daily life. The repression begins here.

**Phase three — re-emergence and the gravesite.** Brian, released, finds the adolescent or young-adult Bruce and forces a confrontation that the canon stages at Rebecca's grave. The encounter is not a reconciliation attempt in any depicted sense; Brian's bearing is aggressive from the first beat. He attacks. In the struggle his head strikes the headstone; he dies. Bruce represses the killing entirely. The memory will not surface until Doc Samson lances it years later (David, #s 376–377).

### Dynamics

Brian's stance toward Bruce is the purest expression of projective identification in the case file. Bruce is the externalized container of everything Brian cannot bear about himself: the contaminated body, the inheritance from his own father, the monstrous core. Every act of cruelty is the defense renewing itself. Every demonstration of Bruce's intelligence is a double wound — confirming the not-human reading, and inflicting the narcissistic injury of being outshone by a child.

There is also envy, which the file should not soften. Brian was a competent physicist; Bruce was, evidently from infancy, going to be a great one. The father saw the trajectory before the child knew he was on it, and hated him for it. The envy is folded into the contamination narrative — the gifts are pathological, not enviable — but the affect underneath is recognizably the older man's resentment of the younger's future.

From Bruce's side, the relationship is the foundation of his entire later psychic structure. The Hulk is, at the simplest level, the body the child wished he had had in the kitchen on the nights his father came home. The Guilt Hulk is Brian's voice introjected. The Devil / Immortal stratum is the household itself, taken inside and made cosmological. Every later identity is a reply to this man.

### Evolution

- **0–2 years:** Bruce as object of revulsion; intermittent direct violence; Brian's hostility largely routed through Rebecca's body, with the infant as audience.
- **2–5 years:** Bruce's precocity emerges; Brian's contempt sharpens; verbal cruelty becomes pedagogically directed ("you are not normal, you are a thing"); direct beatings of the child increase.
- **~6 years:** the murder. Brian's last act of fathering is the coaching of the child into silence — an act that imprints on Bruce both the prohibition against speaking the truth of the family and the conviction that his own testimony does not count.
- **6–teens:** absence. Brian institutionalized. Bruce with Susan. The repression hardens; the father becomes a hole in the world rather than a presence.
- **Adolescence / young adulthood:** the gravesite. Brian initiates; Brian dies; Bruce represses the killing as well as the witnessing.
- **Posthumous, lifelong:** Brian as internal voice. Every Hulk fragmentation, every self-loathing thought-stream, every catastrophic dissociation routes through the introject. The man is more present after his death than during his life.

### Trust / Safety

Trust never existed; safety in his vicinity was zero from infancy. The single most diagnostically grim feature of this relationship — set against the general literature on batterers — is the absence of any depicted positive engagement at all. Most violent fathers have intermittent warm moments with their children, and the warm moments are part of what binds the child to the bond. Brian had none on record. The child was, from birth, conscripted as monster.

### Failure Mode

The relationship's failure mode is the cycle's completion: the abused child, grown, kills the abuser. The canon places this as accidental within the struggle — headstone impact — but the legal and moral status is irrelevant to the psychological structure. Bruce killed his father, repressed it, and built his entire adult identity around the unintegrated fact. Brian, for his part, walked into the encounter behaving exactly as he had behaved when Rebecca tried to leave: confronted with a loved one's autonomy, he attacked. The failure mode was his consistency.

### Leverage

Brian's leverage over Bruce during life was total: physical, definitional, epistemic. He told the child what the child was, and the child believed it for decades. The leverage outlived him because it had been internalized; the introject continues to wield it from inside Bruce's own skull, which is why the lancing of the memory is therapeutically necessary and insufficient — knowing where the voice came from does not silence it.

Bruce's leverage over Brian, never used in life, was the truth. He saw the killing. He could have spoken it. The household's entire latter-period architecture was organized around suppressing this leverage — the threats, the coaching, the post-acquittal disappearance into institutional care.

---

## 3. Brian's Father — The Originating Abuser (Unnamed)

### History

The unnamed paternal grandfather appears in canon as backstory rather than scene (Mantlo, #312; expanded in David's #377 dream-therapy sequence, where Brian's own father is glimpsed as the source of Brian's beatings). He is not given a name, an occupation, a face we can fix. He is the originating wound — the man whose blows produced the boy who became the man who produced Bruce.

He must be filed under Enemies for a structural reason: in Brian's interior life, his father was the figure he was simultaneously running from (the sterilization attempt is a refusal to perpetuate him) and becoming (the abuse of Rebecca and Bruce is the perpetuation). The relationship outlasted the man.

### Dynamics

This is the relationship Brian fought every day, with his fists, against the wrong targets. The defensive structure documented in Section 3 — projective identification, identification with the aggressor, the encapsulated contamination delusion — is in its entirety a response to this man. The "monster" Brian could not bear inside himself is, in clinical translation, the introjected abuser: the father's voice, the father's hand, the father's contempt, taken in by the child Brian and never metabolized.

### Evolution

- **Brian's childhood:** chronic beatings. The behavioral details are not depicted; the structural fact is.
- **Brian's adolescence / young adulthood:** presumed separation (Brian gets out, gets educated, becomes a physicist). The father either dies, is cut off, or simply recedes from the record. The escape is geographic and credentialed; it is not psychological.
- **Brian's adulthood:** the father is dead or absent in the world and fully alive in the interior. Every encounter with Rebecca's tenderness, with Bruce's intelligence, is mediated through the introject.
- **Brian's death:** the cycle terminates one generation later when Bruce kills Brian, repeating the patricidal structure that Brian could never enact against his own father.

### Trust / Safety

Inapplicable to the man; total contamination of Brian's internal world by him. The safety Brian could not produce for his own son was the safety he never received.

### Failure Mode

The intergenerational transmission of abuse, exactly as the developmental-trauma literature describes it. The father's failure mode was reproduced in the son, who reproduced it on his son, who broke the chain — by ending the father physically, and by, very imperfectly and across decades, surviving long enough to begin integrating the introject.

### Leverage

The grandfather's leverage over Brian was the total leverage of the abuser over the small child: he defined what Brian was. Brian spent his life trying to refute the definition and confirming it with every act. The leverage was never relinquished because there was no relationship left in which to relinquish it; the man was inside, and the inside was where Brian fought him, badly, by hurting people the man had never met.

---

## 4. The Institutional Apparatus — Police, Court, Hospital (Collective Adversary / Collective Enabler)

### History

After Rebecca's killing, Brian moves through a sequence of institutions: arresting police, prosecutors, defense, court, and finally a psychiatric facility. The canon's account is compressed but its shape is clear: he beat the legal case (insufficient evidence with the child witness suppressed) and was committed to a mental hospital for a period of years. He emerges; he finds Bruce; he dies.

These institutions function in his life as a single composite adversary-enabler. He fought them — beating the case is an active accomplishment, not a passive escape — and he was held by them, and they failed to either punish or rehabilitate him. He left the system as he entered it: a man with an encapsulated delusional structure, severe alcohol-use disorder, a personality organized around the export of an internal monster, and now an additional layer of grievance against the apparatus that had detained him.

### Dynamics

Brian's behavior toward the institutional apparatus is the behavior of his compensatory self at its most operational. The credentialed, presentable, reality-testing-adequate Brian is the one who beats the case, who performs adequately for the commitment evaluators, who is eventually judged dischargeable. The domestic Brian is sequestered. The bifurcation documented elsewhere in this file is, in the encounter with the institutions, weaponized for survival.

### Evolution

- **Arrest and pre-trial:** the compensatory self is mobilized. He presents as the bereaved/wronged husband, the scientist, the man whose child is too young and too disturbed to testify.
- **Trial:** acquittal or equivalent on the homicide; commitment on mental-health grounds. The legal disposition is in his favor on the criminal axis and against him on the civil-commitment axis, which is the worst possible combination for rehabilitation — he is held without being held responsible.
- **Hospitalization:** the canon does not depict the inpatient course in detail. What is inferable: he performed well enough on the wards to be discharged. Performance on wards is a known behavioral capacity of antisocially-structured patients with intact reality testing in non-delusional domains. His delusion is encapsulated; he can talk around it; he can present as recovered.
- **Discharge:** he leaves with his structure intact and with one new conviction — that he survived the apparatus and is now owed the resumption of his life, including the resumption of his relationship with his son.
- **Post-discharge:** the gravesite confrontation, and death.

### Trust / Safety

He trusted none of these institutions and was, in his terms, correct not to: they tried to take his freedom and partially succeeded. They, for their part, extended him exactly the trust the era's psychiatric standards required and no more — they detained him, they treated him insufficiently, they released him. The safety they provided to Bruce was the safety of distance, which lasted until it didn't.

### Failure Mode

A composite failure: prosecutorial failure to convict on the homicide; psychiatric failure to either diagnose precisely or treat substantively; discharge planning failure to assess his risk to the surviving child witness. The apparatus did approximately what the apparatus of that era and that class context did with such a man: contained him briefly, returned him to society, and was not present at the gravesite.

### Leverage

The institutions' leverage over Brian was the threat of re-commitment, which appears to have constrained him after discharge to the extent of not committing further publicly-visible offenses until the encounter with Bruce — an encounter he could rationalize, plausibly to himself and possibly to a later evaluator, as not of his initiation. Brian's leverage over the institutions was his presentability, his credentials, and the era's willingness to extend benefit of the doubt to a white male research scientist whose only victims were his own wife and child.

---

## 5. Professional Peers — The Los Alamos / Navajo Ordnance Cohort (Structural Rivals, Undepicted Individually)

### History

No individual colleague is named in canon. Brian's professional milieu is referenced by setting — nuclear-physics research, the Los Alamos / Navajo Ordnance ambit, the post-war / Cold War weapons-physics culture — without being populated. This absence is itself diagnostically informative. A surveillance file on a man of his profession would normally surface dozens of named coworkers, project leads, security contacts; the canon's silence is not because they did not exist but because Brian's relational life did not extend to them in any way the narrative needed to mark.

### Dynamics

Inferred structurally. Brian's compensatory self functioned in the workplace; he held a position, he kept a clearance, he was not fired for the drinking. This implies a baseline of professional courtesy, deliverable competence, and the ability to mask. It also implies that the workplace was a theater of chronic, low-grade narcissistic injury for him — every senior colleague was a man he was not yet, every peer's success was a measure of his own contamination, every junior's ambition was a reminder of the future he half-believed he was poisoning. The contamination delusion is not separable from the occupational identity; it is generated *by* it. He believed himself irradiated because he worked with radiation, and the belief gave the self-loathing he already carried a scientific gloss.

### Evolution

- **Early career:** functional, ambitious, presumably promising enough to be hired into restricted research. The compensatory self is at its strongest.
- **Mid-career, around the conception and birth of Bruce:** the delusion crystallizes. Workplace functioning is maintained but the home is collapsing.
- **Post-killing and commitment:** career terminated. No depicted return to physics after discharge. The compensatory self loses its theater; what remains is the domestic tyrant without a household to tyrannize, until Bruce.
- **Post-discharge:** structurally underemployed, presumably; the canon's silence on his vocational life after the hospital is total.

### Trust / Safety

Mutual professional civility, presumed; no relationship of substance to either side; safety neutral within the workplace, because the violence was sequestered to the home.

### Failure Mode

The cohort's failure mode, to the extent they bear any, is the standard failure mode of professional networks confronting a colleague's domestic violence in the mid-20th century: they did not see, they did not ask, they were not configured to intervene. Brian's failure mode within the cohort was that the workplace, which could have been a route of repair — mentorship, sublimation, generative work — was instead recruited only as defensive cover and as fuel for the delusion.

### Leverage

The cohort's leverage over him was the implicit threat of professional consequence for misconduct, which kept the violence inside the house. His leverage over them was nil; he was a competent but unexceptional member of a large enterprise. When he disappeared into commitment, he was replaced.

---

## 6. The Internal Monster — Self-as-Enemy

This sub-section is not a category error. A total-surveillance file on Brian Banner must include the figure with whom he spent the most time, against whom he fought hardest, and whom he could neither leave nor defeat: himself, in the form he could not bear.

### History

Constructed in childhood under the originating father's blows. Elaborated in adolescence as the inheritance dread — *I will become him*. Given pseudoscientific scaffolding in adulthood by the radiation work — *I am contaminated, my gametes are damaged, my offspring will be monstrous*. Acted upon, surgically, in the sterilization attempt. Confirmed, in his perception, by Bruce's existence. Defended against, daily, by alcohol and by the export of the monster into Bruce's body.

### Dynamics

The relationship with the internal monster is the central relationship of his life. Rebecca and Bruce are, in the unkindest accurate framing, recruited into a drama whose only real participants are Brian and his introjected father. The wife is the


## Powers, Abilities & Their Limits

# POWERS, ABILITIES & THEIR LIMITS

## Preamble: A Baseline-Human File in a Powered Universe

Brian Banner has no powers. He is a baseline human male, mid-twentieth-century American, of above-average native intelligence, working in one of the most rarefied technical specialties available to his generation. In a universe whose dossiers routinely catalogue gamma metabolism, Asgardian physiology, and mutant phenotypes, the analytic discipline required here is the opposite of the usual one: not to inventory the spectacular, but to map exactly how much damage a *credentialed, unaltered, sober-enough* human being can do — to a wife, to a child, to a sixty-year textual lineage of consequence — using only the abilities every PhD-holding alcoholic batterer of his cohort possessed. Brian's "powerset" is the powerset of the ordinary domestic tyrant amplified by intellect and shielded by credential. That is what makes his file legible as a weapons inventory rather than a biography.

---

## 1. Intellectual Capacity

**Mechanism.** Native general intelligence well into the upper percentiles; trained in physics to the doctoral level; sustained employment in research environments (Los Alamos / Navajo Ordnance-adjacent) that screened, in his era, for both technical competence and the social presentability required to hold a security clearance. The intellect is the real thing. He is not a fraud, and any analytic frame that treats him as one will misread him.

**Range.** Confined operationally to applied nuclear physics. There is no depicted record of breadth — no humanistic interests, no creative output, no second discipline, no mentorship, no published popularization. The mind is narrow-deep, instrumentalized for technical problem-solving and, in the domestic theater, for the elaboration of his contamination delusion into a pseudo-scientific architecture that the layman wife and small child cannot rebut.

**Limits.** Two, both severe.

First, the intelligence does not extend into self-knowledge. He cannot turn the instrument on his own structure. The same man who can model neutron behavior cannot perceive that his fear of fathering a monster is the inherited voice of his own father; the introspective bandwidth is zero. This is the classic dissociation seen in high-functioning abusers — instrumental cognition preserved, reflective cognition walled off.

Second, the intelligence is brittle under narcissistic injury. Confronted with a son who reads early and does mathematics before school age, his cognitive apparatus does not generate curiosity or pride; it generates the *theory* of the monster-child. The intellect is recruited as the delusion's defense attorney. A more secure mind would have used the same data to build a different conclusion; Brian's mind can only build the one conclusion its substrate permits.

**Growth over time.** None depicted, and structurally none possible. The institutionalization following Rebecca's murder produces no insight; the post-release Brian who confronts the adult Bruce is identical in cognitive-affective organization to the Brian of the early flashbacks. Whatever therapy was attempted slid off a personality structure whose entire purpose is to prevent the relevant recognitions.

**Signature application.** The construction of the contamination narrative — a delusional system rendered in the vocabulary of genetics and radiation rather than of demons or curses, which is precisely what makes it durable, communicable to himself, and unfalsifiable in the home. He weaponizes his credential against his family's epistemic defenselessness. Rebecca cannot argue him out of a position phrased in roentgens.

---

## 2. Professional Functionality and Social Camouflage

**Mechanism.** A learned capacity — almost certainly absorbed in adolescence as survival behavior in his own violent home — to compartmentalize affect and present a regulated public self. He arrives at work on time. He performs tasks. He passes background checks. He does not, on the record, assault colleagues, drink openly on site to the point of dismissal, or display the contamination delusion to supervisors. Whatever rage and paranoia move through the household at night is sealed off at the front door at morning.

**Range.** Sufficient for institutional survival in a structured, hierarchical, technocratic workplace of the 1950s–60s. Probably insufficient for any environment requiring extended unstructured social performance — there is no record of friendships, social hosting, community involvement, religious affiliation, or extended-family engagement. The camouflage is calibrated for the narrow band of professional interaction and collapses outside it.

**Limits.** It is camouflage, not regulation. The capacity is to *withhold* the pathology from a specific audience for a specific interval, not to reduce it. The pressure displaced from the workday is paid back, with interest and a multiplier from alcohol, into the home that evening. This is the engine of the entire intimate-partner violence cycle in his case: the better he performs at work, the more violently the unspent contents discharge at night.

**Diagnostic meaning.** That he *can* hold the line at work proves he is not globally disinhibited and therefore not exculpated by any global condition. He chooses his targets. He chooses his theater. The cruelty is governed by an internal calculus of safety and consequence, which is to say it is volitional in the legally and morally meaningful sense even when it is affectively driven.

---

## 3. Physical Capacity for Violence

**Mechanism.** Adult male body, presumably of unremarkable build for a sedentary academic of his era, but more than sufficient to overpower a woman and a child. No martial training is depicted or implied. The violence is unskilled — fists, grabs, shoves, blunt instruments of opportunity — the autodidact's vocabulary of the home batterer.
